It often happens that blood flows from an artery which is cut at a wound, whether it occurs externally, or at the incision of an abscess, or of a member's cautery, and similar cases; and it becomes difficult to stop it.
When this happens to someone, then quickly reach with your hand to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger on it: and press it strongly until the blood is blocked under your finger, and nothing can escape from it.
Then place small olive cauterials on the fire, and a larger number of them: and blow on them until they are thoroughly ignited.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery is ruptured, and place the cautery on the vein after you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the blood is stopped.
If the blood flows out when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery and extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from those that are prepared in the fire.
Therefore, do not cease to do this, one after another, until the blood is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is there, and do not cause further harm to the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to stop it, especially when the artery is large, unless by one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it has not been extracted. For when it is drawn out, its ends are contracted, and the blood is severed. Or it should be tied with a strong thread. Or let medicines with the property of severing the blood be applied to it, and bind it with a suitable constrictive poultice.
He who administers its severing with a ligature, and binding with cloth, or application of burning substances, and similar methods, does not benefit it entirely, except rarely.
If it happens to someone and a physician or medicine is not present, then begin by placing the index finger over the opening of the vein itself, as we have mentioned, and binding it very tightly until the blood is blocked from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger should not be removed, always cooling it with very cold water until the blood congeals, thickens, and is severed.
And during this time, one should consider what it needs from a cautery or medicine.
 On Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in Wounds, and Similar Matters.
We have already spoken in the first chapter about every sickness in which it is appropriate to use fire for cauterization and burning medicine, its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I have placed there chapters from the top of the head down to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter by the same method: so that it may relieve the seeker of what he seeks.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you to learn, O sons, what is in this chapter concerning the fear which is mentioned in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary to have a more vigorous caution, because in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation with blood often occurs, by which life depends on the opening of a vein, or an incision over an abscess, or the opening of an ulcer, or the treatment of a wound, or the extraction of an arrow, or an incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of which trembling and fear arise, and in many of them death occurs.
And I warn you not to let hesitation overcome you, for sometimes methods of people with the methods of illnesses fall upon you in this art. For there are those among them who become angry with their sickness, and death is And when the illness is from fear which signifies death.
And from them is he who gives his ringing sound and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is deadly.
Therefore, you should not agree with him who comes to you from them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be stronger than your desire and your greed. And do not presume anything from it unless after certain knowledge, which verifies with you that to which a praiseworthy succession arrives.
And use it in the cure of all your ailments, by prognosis and indication of that to which health appropriately leads.
For in that is your assistance for gaining fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you with His understanding, O sons, and not deprive you of direction and inspiration: for that is in His hand.
And I have indeed organized this Chapter by distinguishing it into sections, according to what preceded in the chapter of Cauterization, from the neck down to the feet: so that your search for what you want from it and what you need may be alleviated.
Regarding the treatment of water that accumulates in the heads of children.
Chapter I.
Know that this illness often occurs in children at birth: or when the midwife grasps the child's head without gentleness.
And it sometimes happens also for a hidden cause which is not known.
And indeed I have not seen this disease in anyone other than children: and death hastened to all of them whom I saw; therefore it seemed to me to abandon the treatment of it.
And indeed I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day until he could not sit up straight because of the size of his head; and the swelling increased until he died.
And this moisture indeed gathers either between the skin and the bone: or it gathers under the bone on the surface.
And the operation for this, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then you must make a single incision in the middle of the head in width: and the length of the incision should be about two fingerbreadths, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the form of the spatula.
If the moisture has increased more and the abscess is larger, then make two incisions according to this pattern.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides: and the water is depressed when you press it with your hand towards the interior, and this is not hidden from you: then you must make three incisions in the middle of the head according to this manner.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the wounds with cloths and poultices. Then fumigate it from above with wine and oil up to the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with bandages, and ointments.
And do not leave to bind the head equally. And feed the weak with all dry food of little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is to determine where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of fluid appear.
For sometimes it is more on the back of the head, or on the forehead, or on the right, or on the left.
Therefore, make an incision where the abscess and the accumulation of fluid appear to you: and cut it open as much as possible for you.
And observe, lest you cut an artery, and a flow of blood occurs, and the patient dies from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Regarding the incision of arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When catarrhs affect the eyes or the chest of someone, and it becomes chronic, and the treatment with medicines does not help: then the final treatment for that is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you want to incise them, the patient should shave their head. Then rub the area with a coarse cloth so that the arteries become visible. Then the patient should compress his neck with any cloth. Then observe where the vein pulses. And the locations of both are the depressed areas behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in some people.
Then mark with encaustic on both: and cut each with a scalpel on both sides sharply, the incision reaching the bone: and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery, and cut it upwards with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers joined.
For when the vein is cut, blood exits with a pulsating flow, which leaps into the interior frequently.
If however the artery does not appear to the sense, then it is necessary to measure from the ear a length of three fingers. Then mark with encaustic, and cut until the bone.
And what is necessary to let out of the blood is six ounces and you may emit more or less from it. All of this according to the amount that appears to you from the strength of the patient and the fullness of his artery.
Then attend to the wound. Then if anything remains attached to the bone from the cut, then cut that off, so that a warm abscess does not occur: then bind the wound with strips of linen cloth.
Then care for it with an ointment until it heals.
Regarding the removal of arteries located in the temples.
Chapter III.
When an individual suffers from chronic hemicrania or acute catarrhs due to sharp humidities and heat in the temporal arteries, or severe and chronic soda-like conditions, and similar ailments: and if treatment with medicinal cures does not improve it: then we have found in these illnesses the extraction of arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have narrated.
And the method of the operation for their extraction is to shave the hairs that are on the temples and focus on the visible artery in the temple. For it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is rarely hidden except in a few individuals or due to extreme cold.
If it is hidden from you, then have the patient bind his head with any cloth: then rub the area with a coarse cloth. Or steam the area with hot water until it appears to you with a clear manifestation. Then take the spatula which I previously shaped. Then scrape the skin with it easily until you reach the artery. Then throw the hook into it, and pull it upwards until you extract it from the skin, and make it emerge from the tissues beneath it on all sides.
If however the artery is thin: then wrap it with the tip of the hook. Then cut from it the portion according to the amount by which its ends are extended, and drawn together, so that bleeding does not occur. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not fully stop.
Then evacuate six ounces of blood down to three.
If the artery is large: then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a double, strong thread, of silk: or of the gut of a hen, so that corruption does not set in too quickly before the wound heals, and bleeding occurs.
Then cut the excess that is between the two ligatures. Do this at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery knife up to the mouth: until its ends are cut, and it will remain in place for the same procedure.
And it is better practice, unless the patient has a fever, or has a heated temperament. For cautery is one of those things that helps to stop humors. Therefore, it is better assistance.
And after the extraction of arteries, the place should be filled with old cooked material: and properly made lint should be placed on top.
And after the treatment, it should be cared for with drying medicines that promote flesh growth, and with plasters, until it heals.
If during the operation blood flows from an artery, then quickly proceed to sever it with a cautery, or fill the place with zegi: and hold your hand until the blood is severed. If none of those are available to you, then place your finger on the place until the blood"congeletur sanguis"the blood freezes And embrocate the place with water of intense coldness until the sharpness subsides and is constricted as necessary.
And because it is lighter and easier to extract the artery, you should cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives: let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to almadatem. However, they are much less sharp than the two knives. Because if they are sharp like the two knives, the cold affects both quickly: and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, because there is a slight thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained, and the flesh is cut quickly: with this method, the work is better, lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space between the two knives be the thickness of a finger, after you mark two places with cautery: and place both very hot until they reach the mouth, and cut the vein in two places: so that what is between its ends may be extended. For they do not consolidate completely with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
On the treatment of acute and persistent tears from the eyes.
When the tears are persistent and their course is from the veins that are above the skull of the head externally: and no cure can be effected by medicine with medicaments, and you see the face of the patient already reddened, and he feels a creeping sensation in his forehead, like the creeping of ants: and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that their hairs are already corroded with corrosion: and his eyelids are heated by the sharpness of the tears: then the ancients required this operation for their cure, and they named it cure with the Sword.
And it is that you instruct the patient to shave the hairs that are on his forehead. Then make three equidistant cuts on the forehead along its length: and let the length of each cut be about two fingers, with one cut equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, the second along the length somewhat away from the movement of the eyebrows, and the third from the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries that are on both sides: and keep your hand away from the junction of the two jaws, and let the space of the entire incision be the width of three joined fingers: and have a piece of prepared sponge or many pieces of dried cloth with which you will dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, whose following form is this. From the incision which is at the temple part, to the second incision: and with it, remove all the skin that is between the two incisions, with a knife, which is above the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the second incision. Then remove this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a sharp knife, acute on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached with skin, and its smooth side towards the bone: and push it until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues until what appears from the skin.
Then do this in the middle fissure until the other fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has flowed, press the areas from the clots of blood.
Then place a lint from old cloth in every fissure, and put on them small pieces soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And take care that a warm abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, remove the bandage, and administer a large infusion with warm water. Then treat it with basilicon ointment, and other things with which wounds are treated: until it is healed.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of sharp tears and catarrhs to the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When sharp, pungent, persistent catarrhs happen to someone, and you see his eyes weakened, small, and already their sight is weakened: and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hairs fall out of them, and there is a sharp, painful pain deep in the head, followed by sneezing: then take from these occurrences that those materials and catarrhs do not come except from many veins.
And the better treatment for them is this. And it is that you instruct the patient to rub his forehead. Then make one incision in the middle of the forehead, or another shortly across its width, and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision go up to the bone: and keep your hand away from the jaws near the temples, which move during mastication: until when the bone is exposed: and you will dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with an old cautery, or with threads from linen. Then bind it tightly with bandages: and soak the bandages with wine and oil, so that a warm abscess does not occur.
And when you loosen it and see that the warm abscess is already diminishing: then you must rub the bone until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then care for it with a drying regimen that causes flesh to grow: such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony: and prepare an ointment from them, and administer it for the birth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of that which falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is of one of four types: either a mineral stone, or similar to stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a liquid substance, such as water, vinegar, and things similar to them; or it is some animal.
When a stone or stone-like substance that does not add to or grow in the ear falls into the ear, expose the ear to the sun: if you see the stone, then drip something from violet oil or hyssop oil into the ear. Then administer its extraction and make the person sneeze with costus, and plug the nostrils at the onset of sneezing, after you place pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and pull the ear upward.
For it often exits with this treatment.
If it does not exit, then administer its extraction with Gesti, that is, with fine pizigarolis: which has this form.
If it exits with the use of gesti, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a fine, few-fold blind hook.
If therefore it does not exit through that, then make a tube of copper, and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole very much, and pack what is around the tube with wax softened with oil, so that there is no way for air except through the tube: then extract with your breath strong suction.
For it often exits along with what we have mentioned. If it does not exit this way, then take a small amount of helkalimbat or visco, that is, prepared gluten with which birds are caught, and place it on the end of the probe after you have properly wrapped it with tow: then insert it into the ear hole easily after you dry the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not exit with all that we have mentioned, then hurry to make an incision before a hot abscess or spasms occur.
And the method of the incision is to bleed the patient first in the cephalic vein: and let blood from him according to the measure of his strength. Then have the patient sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near its fatty part in a depressed area: and let the incision be of a crescent shape until you reach the stone; then remove it with whatever instruments are possible for you.
Then cleanse the incision quickly with your own hand, and care for it until it heals.
If the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its removal.
And if it does not obey you to come out, then take a thin, subtle spatula, according to this shape.
And administer with it the incision of that kind from the grains which fall into the ear, and do not do this when you think that grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you place it in many pieces: then extract them with hooks, or with a fine probe, or with suction, as we have said, for its extraction is alleviated.
In the water which enters the ear, the patient must use sneezing with condysium at first: and he has already filled his ear with some old cooked substance: and he is lying on that ear in which there is water. If it exits through that, it is good.
And if it does not: then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, soft, and heat them with fire for a moment, and let the patient insert one of them into the opening of his ear: and jump on one foot from that side, and strike with another stone on the stone which is in the ear. Therefore, he should not stop doing this with stone and stone until all the water comes out.
And now the water exits in such a way that you take one from a parpyro or from a reed, and insert one end of it into the ear: and light the other end with fire until most of it is burned. Then use another reed, and do this many times until all the water comes out: and extract it with a tube as was mentioned before regarding the stone.
In the extraction of an animal entering into it, if it is of a small body, like a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of a large body, which appears to the sense: then administer its extraction with hooks and forceps; and its matter is easier than all those held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division and in the treatment of distillations, and your cure does not take effect: then you must look at the ear in the sun. If therefore something of the worms appears there, then extract it with hooks or with subtle forceps.
But if nothing appears there, then take a tube, which has this form, narrow below, wide above: and insert the thin end into the ear according to the amount that the patient can tolerate it: then suck with a strong suction. Do this many times until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then smear the tip of the tube with wax, as I said with the stone.
If they do not come out with what we have said, then administer the distillations which the ancients have already used to kill worms: and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your effusion, with which you pour oil and medicines into the ear, be done with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, wide above.
Let it be that the impelling device which is in the concavity of the tube be properly made from bronze. And if you wish, take a rod, and wrap cotton at its end with a proper wrapping; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever medicine you choose into the tube: and place it in the ear; then insert the rod with the cotton above, and press it with your hand moderately so that the oil is propelled into the concavity of hearing, and the patient feels it internally: and what is infused into the ear should already be warmed slightly by fire: and beware that what is distilled into it is not very cold: for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
Regarding the treatment of auricular ailments due to opilation.
Sometimes infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it has also happened to some individuals that opilation in the ear canal occurred due to a wound or flesh grown in it.
And this opilation sometimes occurs in the deep part of the ear canal, which is not visible: and it sometimes affects the outer part of the canal slightly, which is visible.
Therefore, that which occurs in the deep part of the ear and which is not visible, is in most cases difficult to heal.
And in that which is visible, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and look into it: then if you see an apparent opilation, open it with a fine spatula, whose form is as follows, let its end have some small width, part of which is sharp: and the rest of the spatula be of two smooth sides: so that it does not hurt the ear.
If therefore the growth is from flesh born in the ear canal: then hold it with a fine hook, and cut it with vehement ease, until all the flesh is removed.
If the growth is in the depth of the ear: then take a fine, gentle probe, and heat it slightly with fire: then insert it into the ear canal.
If therefore you sense a growth with it, which the patient finds with heaviness of hearing, attempt to open it with ease: and beware lest you wound the nerves of the ear.
Then apply to the ear lint according to the size of the opening which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment: do this for several days until you are sure of the wound's healing.
Or take lint, and soak it in rose water, and sprinkle ground zedoary on it, and use it.
If you see the ear already forming an abscess: then it is necessary to remove the lint, and allow it with another piece of lint which you wrap in cerote made with rose oil, until the warm abscess settles: then care for it until it heals.
If a flow of blood should occur: then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water, and place it on the affected ear: and use the remaining treatment which stops the flow of blood as described in the chapter on distillations.
Regarding the treatment of Altheli of the eyelids.
Chapter VIII.
Injuries that occur on the eyelids of the eye are sometimes moist and are dry.
Therefore, you must hold them with forceps: or with a hook, and cut them with a spatula from their roots. Then place ground zegi on the affected areas.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lentil-shaped cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
On the treatment of warts on the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter Nine.
Often something happens on the eyelids that resembles warts in its strength and hardness, and because of this it is called a granum: and it is an aggregation of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
The operation on them is to examine whether there is a granum protruding from the eyelid, which appears and moves in all directions, and its removal is easy.
Therefore, make an incision over it lengthwise. Then remove it from all sides with ease until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
If the excision is not prepared for you unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a temporary incision, then it harms the patient somehow.
If the fissure is large, then join it with stitches, and care for it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For the ointment restores it and causes flesh to grow over it.
And if the stone is inclined towards the inner part of the ear towards the internal surface, then invert the eyelid and suspend the stone with a hook; besides which you may need an incision, and press it from all sides.
If the eyelid is extended by an incision, it does not harm in any way.
Then wash the eye after the stone is cut out with salt water, and care for the area with something that causes flesh to grow over it until the patient heals.
On the treatment of Axirnath of the eyelids.
Chapter Ten.
Axirnath is fat that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid. It mostly affects children, weighing down their eyes and causing them to have colds in the mornings. They cannot look at the light of the sun because tears rush to their eyes, and you see them always lying on their faces and sleeping on them. Their eyelids beneath the eyebrows are wet, covered by swelling, and the prominence of Axirnath is visible to the eye. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the sick person puts his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth, and prepare wax on it, and make a circle out of that wax according to the size that contains Axirnath on all sides. Then place him on it, and press with your fingers on all sides, so that Axirnath gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture with a scalpel penetrating fissures horizontally, and let not the fissure be larger than that which is made in bloodletting deeply.
Or it is necessary to cut through the entire skin until you reach Axirnath, for in many conditions Axirnath proceeds from a fissure according to the shape of a fragment of white fatty tissue.
Therefore, extract it with a cloth, which you infiltrated between your fingers to the outside: and you turn your hand to the right and left until it disperses: and beware, lest you add to the fissure, so that you do not reach the eye with the scalpel and harm it.
If Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, you should extend the fissure a little with ease until Axirnath appears. Then extract it as we have said.
Then soak the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the area, and bind it with bandages.
And there are some people who rub salt and place it in the concavity of the fissure, so that what remains of that moisture may dissolve.
Then care for it until it heals.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs in the place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
Regarding the methods for ataxmia of the eye, that is, when the eyelid softens, causing it to droop more than it should.
Chapter XI.
When additional hairs grow on the eyelids beyond the course of the natural hairs and become old: then they harm the eye and cause various illnesses to occur, such as continuous tears, softening of the eyelids, and opacities, until it becomes a cause for the destruction of the eye.
And ataxmia of the eye is treated in four ways. Or by cauterization with fire. Or with sharp medicine, according to what was mentioned earlier in the chapter on cauterization. Or ataxmia is treated by incision and suturing. Or by means of tubes, as I shall explain.
It is necessary to place the head of the patient in your lap. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If it turns, that is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, at the part where there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the thread from above, and place it near the hairs themselves, that is, near the margin of the eyelid where the hairs grow: and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a probe.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with the spatula inserted from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then extract the thread, and place a small piece of felt or cloth under the eyelid; then mark above the eyelid with branding iron, which should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it must be done according to the size and elevation of the eyelid: for it already varies among people.
For there are some who need a large quantity to be cut from the eyelid, according to the amount that is softened from the eyelid.
Then with the spatula find above the two lines which you marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct, and let the cut be near the natural hairs, like the thickness of a ray.
Then insert the hook into one of the two corners of the skin. Then scrape the entire area. Then sew together the two lips with a needle and fine wool thread: and clean the blood, and make adhere what remains of the threads above both eyebrows, with some adhesive substances, if you wish to do so: and if not, do not mind.
Then leave the stitches and threads for three days, or four: afterwards care for it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without stitches, and care for it with something that dries and constricts. For the eyelid is raised at the sealing of the wound and its gathering together: but it will be better.
The ancients taught this method of treatment.
However, there is careful attention on the patient, and it is from good practice, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir as well: and it is to mark on the eyelid a figure that resembles the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate or joined hooks, according to this following form, then cut away what is excessive with small scissors, according to this form, with equal incisions.
If it is not possible for you to hold the hook and it does not align: then take a needle with thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure: and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct: and gather the threads between your fingers equally.
Then raise your hand with equal lifting: and raise the skin on which the line is marked, as we have narrated.
Then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures: and care for it until it heals.
If an abscess occurs at your incision or fissure: treat that abscess with cerote and similar soothing ointments.
And it sometimes happens to the eyelid that its hairs turn inward.
Therefore, you should use what we have said in making the incision; but sewing up both is more skillful. On the treatment of Ataxmir with reed.
And it is such that you turn the eyelid and tear open the slit which is inside, as I have described. Then skillfully make two reeds or two fine sticks: the length of which should be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width should be less than the width of a spatula: and you will now cut at the ends of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully place each one over the excess of the eyelid, and tightly bind the two reeds on both sides with a strong tie, and leave it for several days. For skin that is tightly bound will die, and turn black, and rot, until it falls off by itself.
If it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then care for it until it heals.
When it is consolidated, lift the eyelid: and the hairs do not pierce.
And this is the form of the two reeds that you will make according to this figure, and the size in length and width.
Chapter XII.
On raising the piercing hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation is to take a fine needle and insert into it a thread made of fine, soft silk. Then join the ends of it, and tie each end with a very fine knot; and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then arrange another fine thread under it briefly, and knot the ends of it if you wish.
Then let the patient place his head in your lap, and be near the sun so that the operation may be clear; for it is a fine or minute operation.
Then insert the needle with silk into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you draw it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair above the silk: and insert that hair into the silk: whether it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair comes out with the silk in the eyelid with the natural hair.
If the silk is pulled out and the hair does not come out with it: pull the silk downwards with the thread you composed in it until the silk comes out from the upper opening, and see it with your eyes: then pull the silk and the thread together and extract both, and close the eye: leave it closed for two days or three until the hair adheres to its place and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either attach to it a long hair from the natural hairs and lift with it, or leave it until it grows longer over time, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati occurs in the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental therefore occurs from the healing of a wound, or a cut, or a cautery, and similar to these.
And the method of the operation is to split that healing, and to separate its lips, and place between them a strip of linen, and bind it until it heals.
And it is not appropriate to use in its treatment things that dry and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: rather use in it things that soften, such as Greek fennel: and foment with water, in which marshmallow and flaxseed have been cooked: and an unguent of dyaquilon, which has been melted with some oil: and wrap it with a strip of linen, and treat it thus.
And the treasure of your treatment is to strive with all ingenuity that it not heal solidly, according to the form it had at first.
And this operation which we have mentioned is only a correction of Alxatrati, so that the form of the place does not return entirely as it was.
The treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards towards the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs below is truly named Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental occurs from a wound, or a cut, or a cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operation is to take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, making it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread comes out at both ends of the flesh.
Then stretch the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a broad spatula. If the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take a rod, and place it in the cut, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two cuts on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two cuts be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and form an angle: until when joined they form this shape, which is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp lower side is from what follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts: sew it with wool thread.
Then treat them with what we have said about softening medicines and plasters until they heal.
And if an Alxatrati occurs from a cut, and stitching, or cauterization: then it is necessary to make a flat cut under the eyelash hairs, also according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with an ointment, according to what we have said.
And the summary of the discussion on the treatment of Alxatrati, whether it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed accordingly, according to how it is prepared from the form of Alxatrati.
For it is indeed of many diversities in form: and the wise artisan prepares the method with whatever way is possible for him, until he restores the figure to its natural form, or near it, and the patient himself, according to what is suitable for him from the operation and instruments in many dispositions.
Chapter XV.
Regarding the adherence of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid becomes consolidated with the white of the eye and prevents it from performing its natural functions.
Therefore, you must introduce the tip of a probe under the eyelid and lift it up; or extend it with a hook: then cut through the adherence with a blunt spatula that is not sharp like a cutting spatula, but rather slightly blunted: so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula does not injure the eye with its sharpness: and let your incision be such that you pare down that adherence easily until when the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adherence heals.
Then pour salty water into the eye, wash it with water: or drip onto it from a sieve made from dissolved zinc and water.
Then separate between the eyelid and the eye with flaxen oil: and place wool soaked in egg white over the eye: and after the third day use a sieve, and with soldering materials, until it heals.
On the incision of the nail and the prominence of the flesh of the tears.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the nail is according to two modes. Or it is nervous: and it is similar to a hard, fine sieve. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white moisture: which when it touches hot iron, or you try to take it with a hook, it breaks apart, and the hook does not hold onto it.
And neither kind begins unless from the larger lacrimal gland, extending gradually until it covers the vision and prevents light and eye movement.
And the method of operating in its incision is: that the patient places his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a hook of few twists, and extend it upward. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hairs of a horse, an ox, or a cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle for a moment, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upward: and scrape with the hair the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you were sewing it with the hair to its end.
Then cut the remainder in the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural tear flesh, so that the steady flow of tears does not fail from its cutting.
And sometimes the claw is extended with a hook alone: or with a thread, as we have said. Then it should be scraped with a gentle spatula, which has this shape.
And beware that the spatula does not touch the horny layer, and a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil be perforated.
Then after the incision, throw some ground salt into the eye, or something made from ziniar, and leave it until the next day. Then care for it with what is necessary until it heals.
If the claw is not nervous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor can a hook be fixed in it, then this kind of claw is often cured with medicines, and heals as I have described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the patient's eye, and take a fine, gentle spatula of weak sharpness according to this shape. And scrape the claw gently from above with delicacy. If you see that it becomes rarefied and breaks, and the scraper impresses upon it, then drip into the eye from your hour something made from ziniar, or red powder, or ground salt: and bandage the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the operation on it until all of it is removed. Unless there is a warm aposteme contrary to you in the eye: then leave it alone and treat the aposteme until it heals.
Then repeat the operation on it reasonably until it heals.
However, if the prominence of the lacrimal flesh harms the eye greatly, it should be suspended with a hook and a part of it cut off: and do not make the incision at the end, so that the course of the tears is not hindered.
Then apply a red salve to the lacrimal area, or one made from zincwort, until it heals.
Regarding the incision of Alguardenic, and the flesh grown in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in the eyes of some people, red flesh is formed or positioned over until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or extends over the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles a damask rose.
And the operation on it is such that the patient should place their head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks: and hold it with one forceps or tweezers.
Then cut the flesh initially until it is all finished with a cutter. And be careful of the eye so as not to harm it during the operation: and let your cut, whether you make it with a spatula, which we mentioned in the nail incision, or with small scissors, with which asbel is collected, according to how its form will come after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is completed and all the almond milk is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt: or sprinkle on it red powder, and similar things from corrosive medicines: and place over the eye outwardly a cloth with egg white: so that it may be secured from a warm abscess.
If any of the almond milk remains and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a warm abscess: then leave the eye alone, and treat it with that which soothes the abscess.
Then repeat the procedure on it until it is healed.
And similarly treat the added flesh that occurs in the eye of this kind.
However, it is necessary to avoid the treatment of any weak eye by natural means or by accidental means, using iron: and do not intervene with your hand in any of these operations until you see that the member can tolerate that operation.
Regarding the extraction of Sebel from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Sebel are red veins that form over the eye and prevent it from functioning properly: and they weaken the eye over time.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider whether the eye in which Sebel is present is strong and has no other illness besides Sebel: then extract its Sebel.
And it is necessary that you instruct the patient to place their head in your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your judgment: and let the hook be delicate of folding, according to this form, or let there be two hooks set in one body, according to this form.
Then collect with delicate scissors those veins with subtlety, and cleanse the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye, and they dissolve: and beware lest you injure the eye with the ends of the spatula.
And let your operation be at noon facing the sun, and be firm in your operation very much: so that you cut only those veins.
And when you have completed, then distill into the eye green vitriol or red: so that with its sharpness it corrodes what remains of sebel.
If, however, it is not possible for you to collect all of it in that hour, then apply a poultice to the eye with something that alleviates the pain of the eye: and leave it for several days until its pain subsides: and it should be protected from warm abscesses: then repeat on it according to the same method until it heals.
And this is the form of the scissors.
Regarding the reduction of Arissati to the nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians name Arissati the Fistula, which you treat with a cautery or with a sharp burning medicine, according to what was previously described.
And if it is not healed, then there is no efficacy in it: unless you incise above the abscess at its origin, and extract all the moisture and pus within it until the bone is exposed.
When therefore you uncover the tooth, and see in it corruption or blackening, then scrape it with an instrument whose form is this.
And it is called Head of Asper.
Let it be made of Indian iron, and let its head be round like an awl, which should be pointed with the point of a file, or with a subtle point of aliskilferi: and place it on the corrupted part of the bone.
Then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand for a moment until you know that the corruption has been scraped away: and do this several times.
Then restore the area with drying and astringent medicines.
If therefore the area consolidates and flesh grows there, and the course of pus is stopped: and it remains for forty days without recurring, without abscessing, and no accident befalls it: then you know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no skill involved, except to redirect the fistula to the nostril opening, according to this form.
And it is such that you uncover from the second bone with iron or with sharp medicine: and when the bone is uncovered, then take a drill according to this form, let the iron end be triangular: and its wood should be a tapered pyramid down to the end, as you see.
Then place it on the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct: and move your hand slightly away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the awl until it penetrates the mouth, and let the patient feel it: by this he finds air coming out of it when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with astringent drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore the flesh is born and the place hardens: then the matter which was poured out externally returns to the nose: thus it is a milder condition for the patient.
Regarding the reduction of a protrusion of the Eye.
Chapter Twenty.
When the eye protrudes entirely and no harm or reduction occurs in vision: then you must calm the patient: then bleed him: afterwards apply cupping to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then apply to the eye an unguent made from acacia, aloes, frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then bandage the eye over the plaster with many poultices.
And the method of the poultices is as follows: take soft cloths, many of them, and fold each cloth over four times: make many of them: then place them one on top of another according to the amount needed by the eye: then bind them tightly with a hard cloth in a strong bandage.
And repeat cupping with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping and leave the eye tightly bandaged day and night.
If however the bandage is softened again, then it must be tightened greatly; then remove the bandage after a day and night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye has returned to the grape skin, it is good: and if not, then apply the poultice, and the powder, and the compression, and the cupping, until it heals.
Regarding the incision of the grape skin.
Chapter Twenty-One.
When a disruption occurs in the grape skin layer, then it protrudes and extends beyond the eyelids like a grape seed, and the person's appearance is marred because of it, and you wish to cut it: then the procedure for this is as I narrate: and it is to insert a needle at the root of the grape seed from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle with double thread from the lacrimal part, and make it penetrate: and leave the first needle in its place.
Then cut the double thread, and tie it around the grape part with the needle: and tighten it greatly.
Then remove the needle, and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye: and leave it until the thread falls off with the grape.
Then care for the eye with what strengthens it until the wound heals.
And sometimes the grape is tightened on all sides with a circle made of cloth until the eye develops a large protrusion.
Then it should be pierced with a fine spatula that reaches the depth of the eye, and let the whitish moisture flow out, and let the eye sink in, and immediately proceed: then press it until it heals.
On the treatment of Alcomnati.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
This sickness which is called Alcomnati is not anything but a pus that gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operating on it is to have the patient sit on a prepared seat or stand straight.
Then take his head in your hands from two sides and move it until you see the pus come to the lower parts of your eyes. Then it should be fixed, and not allowed to descend, and the light should be restored: and the patient should see things as he saw before: and if it does not go down further, you know that it is water.
If the pus does not prepare to descend with what we have said, then have the patient sit between your hands. Then take a fine spatula and use it to puncture slightly above the corneal membrane at the junction of the conjunctiva with the corneal layer in the crown, until the pus comes out.
When it comes out, then drip warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey: or water in which you have already boiled Greek hay and honey: then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
On the treatment of water descending into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already spoken about the types of water in division, and what is suitable for piercing, declaring and explaining it, then accept the same according to health.
Then it is necessary for you to make the patient sit between your hands, facing the light, near the sun: and bind his healthy eye tightly, and hold it very firmly: then with your right hand if the eye with water is the left eye, or with your left hand if the eye is the right one, hold the eyelid of his eye.
Then take with your right hand the needle if the eye is left: or with your left hand if the eye is right.
Then place the tip of the needle near the cornea at the thickness of the radius in the apparent white of the eye of the smaller lachrymal: then push the needle with force: and you turn your hand with it until it penetrates into the white of the eye: and you will feel that the needle has reached the empty space.
And it is necessary that the measurement of the needle's journey to the depth be the measure of the space from the pupil to the end of the black part: and this is the cornea of the eye. For you will see it in the pupil of the eye itself, because of the clarity of the corneal membrane.
Then place the needle until it reaches the place where the water is. Then press it below once after another.
If water descends immediately, then the patient sees what he opens his sight to immediately, and the pupil in his eye.
Then rest for a little while. If water descends, then remove it secondly, except for the pupil to emerge.
When it is fixed and does not rise: then extract the pupil with ease: and you turn your hand gradually.
Then dissolve something from clear gem salt in water: and wash the eye inside with it. Then place outside the eye the entire piece of cotton, or wool soaked in rose oil, and goose grease: and bind the healthy eye with it.
We, and those of our time, put ground cinnamon with egg white on it.
If the pupil does not obey you for entering into the eye because of its hardness, since it is from a person whose eye is very hard: then you must take a spatula, which is called Alberid, which has this form.
Therefore, insert with it only the connecting part, and do not put your hand deeply into the hole: and you do this only to make a way to the delicate place of the pupil.
Then insert the pupil as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, a properly made resting place should be prepared for the patient, in which he may sleep on his back in a dark room: and he should be prevented from all movements, and from coughing: and give him food that suits his nature: and he should not move his head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its arrangement up to the third day.
Then dissolve it in that dark house, and test its vision: and make it see things. Then reduce the bandage up to the seventh day.
And it should not be done at the time of healing, or immediately after the incision: rather, it should be delayed because water rises quickly through a vehement gaze.
If a hot aposteme occurs, then it is necessary to dissolve the eye before the seventh day: and correct it with what sedates the aposteme until it is calmed: then restore its vision, and place over its face an oral device which regulates its sight under it for several days, while being in that dark house.
Then let him gradually leave the house and move about in his own rooms.
And know that in zaltea the student is not excused from seeing several times: then he may dare to perform the operation.
And indeed it has come to our attention from some who are from Alayrach, who said that in Alayrach a magdan was pierced through which water is drawn.
Therefore, I have not seen anyone in your land who has done this: nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients: and it is possible that this is new.
And these are the forms of the images from the magda, so that one who is ignorant may stand over them.
They shall be made of suitable metal: and their ends should be shaped with this same fineness into triangles, and the end sharp.
Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of flesh protruding in the nose.
Sometimes diverse flesh growths appear in the nose, some of which resemble many-footed scorpions: and from these is a cancerous, stony, dark-colored flesh: and there is also a softer flesh that is not dark-colored.
In those among these flesh growths that are soft, not deceptive, nor cancerous, you must make the patient sit between your hands facing away from the sun, and open his nostrils, and insert a hook into those flesh growths: then pull them outwards, and cut what you have grasped with a fine sharp spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed.
If any part remains that cannot be cut off: then scrape it with one of the fine instruments easily, until nothing of it remains. If blood dominates you, or a hot abscess occurs: then oppose it with what is appropriate; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then quickly cauterize it until the blood is severed and all the flesh is removed.
Then after the incision, vinegar, and water, or wine should be applied to the nose. If the nose is opened and moisture flows from it to the palate, then you know that it is now healed.
If the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that flesh has grown inside within the upper nasal bones to which the instrument does not reach with incision.
Then therefore it is necessary to introduce a flax thread which has some thickness, and many knots on it: and place between each knot the length of a finger, or less: and the patient will manage to insert one end of the thread into his nose with a probe, or with whatever is possible for him after he makes it similar to azzer: and he should pull it through until it reaches the hollow, and exits above his throat.
And indeed boys in schools often do something similar to this: and it is easy for one who wishes.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which comes out over the nose, and the other which remains in the nose. Then cut the flesh with the knots that are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then withdraw the thread: and insert into the nose after wiping away the blood a lint that you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or longer, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then place a lead tube at the end of the matter into the nose for several days until it heals.
If however it requires a treatment that dries, use that.
For making the head purgation device through which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or bronze: so that it is like a small lamp, open like a shell: and its spout similarly.
And if you want to make a closed tube, like a cane.
And the shell of the head purgation device should be full, open: to which there is a handle at the end of it, with which you hold it when you heat oil or any juice and liquid substances in it.
Chapter XXV.
Often a thorn grows at the end of the nose, and it enlarges and increases with days until its appearance becomes ugly.
And because of this, it is necessary to cut it down at the beginning of its appearance and root out all of it. Then place on the area either a cautery or a burning medicine that serves in its place.
If the cutting is delayed until it enlarges, then examine if it is hard, stone-like, dark in color, and insensitive: then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous tumor.
For I have often seen that cutting such a tumor on a person results in great harm to the one who had it.
And if the swelling is of mild touch, not of dark color, and you see that an incision can be made in its entirety, then evacuate the diseased part, and cut it without fear, without dread: and treat the area with what dries and constricts until it heals.
Chapter XXVI.

Know that when there is a separation of continuity in one of these cartilages, the operation avails little, except in certain individuals.
Therefore, when something of that sort happens to someone, you should consider whether it is a fresh wound with its own blood: and then bring together the edges of the wound with a suture: then care for it until it heals.
And if the edges of the separated continuity are already apart, and each part has become healed: then it is necessary to remove all the skin from its visible surface until it bleeds. Then bring together the two edges with a suture, and tighten them, and powder dragon's blood and frankincense over it: and place a piece of diafinicon plaster over the powders, and another of consolidating plasters: and leave it bound for two or three days. Then loosen it, and change the medicine: and leave it until the threads fall off by themselves. Then care for it with an ointment until it heals.
The method of suturing is to bring together the separation of continuity, either with needles, as we have mentioned in the suturing of the abdomen: or bring together the separation with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
Chapter on the removal of lip knots.
Many people often develop hard swellings similar to grains of herb-like growths inside their lips, and some of them are smaller while others are larger.
Therefore you must turn the lip, and cut over all its knot, and suspend it with a hook, and cut it from all sides.
Then fill the place after the incision with ground zegi until the blood is stopped.
Then it should be mixed with vinegar, and the area where there is astringency should be treated with it until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
About the incision of the flesh growth above the gum.
Often flesh grows above the gum, which the ancients call abuluss.
Therefore, you must suspend it with a hook: or hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and let the pus flow, or the blood.
Then place ground zegi or other astringent, drying powders on the area.
If that flesh returns after treatment, for it often returns, then cut it a second time and cauterize it: for it does not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
About the treatment of teeth with iron.
Sometimes on the surfaces of teeth, both from the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, rough, ugly crusts form, and they sometimes blacken and turn yellow or green until corruption from them reaches the gum, and the teeth become foul because of this.
Therefore you must have the patient sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the mill and tooth where the crusts appear to you like sand: until nothing remains of them, and do similarly with the blackness, and greenness, and yellowness, and others until they are cleansed.
If therefore what is at the beginning of the reasoning in them is removed, it is good: and if not, then apply reasoning to them again on another day, secondly, and thirdly, until the study finally reaches what you desire.
And you should know that a tooth requires razors of various forms and many shapes: according to how it is prepared for your operation: therefore, the razor with which the tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the razor with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms.
And these forms of razors should be prepared for you, which you see.
Chapter Thirty.
Regarding the extraction of painful teeth.
You must treat the tooth for its pain with all ingenuity and delay in its extraction. For there is nothing to replace it when it is extracted, since it is a substance of high value: until there is no excuse for its extraction, then you must extract it when the patient is compelled, so that the painful tooth may be secured and certified by you.
For often the pain deceives the patient, and they think that it is in the tooth saha and extract it: then the pain does not cease until the diseased tooth is extracted.
For we have already seen this from the procedure of the bloodletters.
When therefore it is confirmed with you that the painful tooth itself, then you must cut around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is some virtue, until the gum loosens from all sides; then move it with your fingers, or with delicate forceps at first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place the large forceps firmly on it, and hold the head of the patient between your knees; which you have now held firmly so that it does not move: then pull the tooth straight out, so as not to break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under it on all sides with ease, and try to move it as you did at first.
If however the tooth is perforated or corroded: then you must fill that hole with cloth: and hold it tightly with the tip of a slender iron, so that it does not break when you grasp it with forceps.
And you must act carefully with the incision around the gum on all sides extensively.
And guard your strength so that you do not break it, leaving a part behind, which may return to harm the patient with an even greater pain than before.
And beware that you do not do what ignorant bloodletters do in their haste and boldness in extracting it, besides administering what we have mentioned.
For they often cause great infirmities in people, which are harder to heal, leaving all or some of the roots behind, or extracting it along with some bones of the jaw; as we have testified many times.
Then it should be treated after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If a flow of blood occurs from the places (for it often happens), then grind something from zegi, and fill the area with it: and if not, cauterize it if zegi is not available to you.
The forms of delicate forceps with which the tooth is moved should have long ends, a short handle, thick, so that they do not fold when pressed on the tooth: they should be like you see with thick handles, so that when you grip with them, they do not bend themselves nor double over: short ends, and they should be made of Indian iron or properly made steel, straight ends: and at their ends there should be teeth, some of which enter into others and grasp with a proper, strong constriction.
And sometimes the ends are made in the shape of the mouth of a bird which is called a stork, and they have a strong constriction.
Chapter Thirty-One.
Regarding the extraction of teeth and the removal of fractured jawbones.
When something remains after the extraction of a tooth that is broken, then it is necessary to place cow dung with butter on the area for one day or two days until the area softens: then introduce algasti or forceps, whose ends resemble the mouth of a bird which is called a stork. This is the form of the forceps.
Let their ends be made like a file inside: or like aliskilfegi.
If therefore it does not obey you to exit with these forceps, then it is necessary that you cut above the root, and expose all the flesh, and insert the instrument which is called little lever, whose form is this.
Let the end be short and somewhat thick: and let it not be hollow, so that it does not break.
If therefore the root exits through it, it is good: and if not, it is necessary to assist with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The form of the first triangular end, in which there is some thickness.
The form of the thin triangular end.
And with other instruments and tools which have been mentioned earlier in the discussion of teeth.
It is also helpful with this instrument which resembles a large hook, of this form, a twisted triangular end, in which there is some thickness, so that it does not break: and let it not be hollow.
And you should know that dental instruments are many: and similarly other instruments are not counted, and a wise and skilled craftsman discovers the instrument for himself according to what the operation and the illnesses themselves signify.
Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not describe an instrument, due to the diversity of their forms.
If however the bone is fractured from the jaw, or from other bones of the mouth, or it becomes putrefied: then examine it thoroughly in its place with what is suitable for it from the instruments and forceps which I have mentioned in the extraction of roots, and assist with a lever, whose form is this.
Let there be something thick in it gradually, so that it is tightened, or the mouth holds onto it, and does not slip away and go until the mouth comes out: and restore the place with medicines appropriate for that.
If there is a small rot in the bone, then scrape it from its rot, and care for it until it heals.
Chapter XXXII.
About the treatment of prominent teeth above others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, the appearance is marred by this: and especially when it happens in women and girls, that is, in maidens.
Therefore, you should consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth and does not fit, or its correction is not possible, nor its grinding: then extract it. And if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose form is this.
It is similar to a small beak and is made of hardened iron with a very sharp end.
And let your cut, with which you cut it, be done over many days, because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another of the teeth is not disturbed.
If it is prominent and suitable for grinding, then grind it with a file made of iron, whose form is this.
Let it be entirely of iron, and its handle of small strong points, like the file with which needles are made: with which the tooth is gradually ground over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not disturbed and falls out: then smooth it, at the end scrape it with some razors.
If it is a tooth from which a part is broken and it injures the tongue: then one must trim it until the roughness of that break is removed and leveled: and flattened, so that it does not injure the tongue nor corrupt speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the rotation of teeth with gold wires.
When it happens that the front teeth are shaken and jolted by a blow or fall: and the patient cannot bite on what he eats so it does not fall out: and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and the treatment was not effective: then the method is to bind them with gold wire: because silver turns green and rots after some days: but gold never changes.
And let the wire be of medium thickness according to the amount that fits between the teeth.
And the form of the rotation is to take the wire and insert its double between two healthy teeth: then weave with the two ends of the wire between the loose teeth, whether one or more, until you reach with the thread all the way to the healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving back to the part where you started: and hold firmly with skill and wisdom until it is not moved at all: and let your constriction when you bind the wire be at the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
Then cut the excess ends of the wire with scissors: and bring both together, and turn them with pincers: and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth so that they do not injure the tongue: then leave it bound tightly as it is.
If it is loosened or cut, tie them with another thread, and it will remain thus for the whole time.
And this is the form of teeth, and the figure of the arrangement of two sound teeth, and two moving teeth, as you see.
And it sometimes happens that one or two teeth return to their places after falling out, and they are retained as we have mentioned, and remain: and this is done only by an artisan who is wise and experienced.
And sometimes a piece of cow's bone is fitted: and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in position, whereby the tooth is supported and held, as we have said: and it remains, and serves for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
Sometimes this ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from an injury that has already become consolidated.
And the operation is to open the mouth of the patient in your hand, and lift his tongue: then cut that nervous ligament across until the tongue is freed from its restraint.
If there is something hard and knotted in it, and this is due to the consolidation of an injury: then insert a hook into it, and tear it across until you cut the ligament and loosen the knot.
And be careful that the cut is not deep into the flesh, and that you do not cut the artery there, causing a flow of blood.
Then it should be sewn at the trace of the incision with rose water, or with vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a piece of linen that the patient holds throughout the night: so that it does not harden secondly.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs, then place ground zegi on the area. If the blood overcomes you, then cauterize the area with a mottled cautery, which is appropriate for it: then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes it happens that a swelling occurs under the tongue which resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function: and it may grow so large that it fills the mouth.
And the procedure is to open the patient's mouth facing the sun: and examine the swelling.
If you see that it is of a brown or black color, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it: then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it tends towards whiteness, in which there is moisture, then insert a hook into it, and cut it with a fine spatula, and free it from all sides. If the blood overcomes you during your procedure, then place ground zegi on it until the bleeding stops: then return to your procedure until you remove it completely.
Then it should be treated with vinegar and salt: and care for it with the rest of the appropriate treatment until it heals.
On the treatment of abscesses of the two tonsils.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
Sometimes there occur between the throat glands, which resemble algodad occurring externally: and they are called the two tonsils: which when you treat with what we have said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, of dark color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of red color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it with iron either, fearing a flow of blood, but rather leave it until it ripens. And then either you will pierce it: or it will burst on its own.
And if it is of white color, round, and its root is thin: then it must be cut open.
And the procedure is such that you should consider before performing it whether the warm abscess has completely subsided or decreased somewhat: then make the patient sit facing the sun, and let his head be in your lap: and open his mouth, and have the servant take between his hands, and press his tongue down with an instrument of this form.
Let it be made of silver or bronze, which should be thin like a knife.
When therefore you press with it the tongue, and the swelling is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and draw it outwards as much as you can, besides which pull out some of the tissues with it.
Then cut it with an instrument, which has the following form. It is similar to scissors: however, the ends of it are curved, so that the opening of each of them is directly opposite the other's sharp end: hence be careful that it is made of iron indoo, or alfuled soaked in.
If this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, which has the following form. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same type of incision.
Then after the incision, the patient should gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds have been boiled: or myrtle leaves, and similar astringents, until the bleeding stops: then care for it until it heals.
And at times, other swellings arise in the throat besides the two tonsils. Cut them accordingly as I have said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And indeed I have treated a woman with an abscess that had formed between her throat, which was drawing it to darkness, loss of senses, which was nearly closing her throat: and the woman was breathing narrowly from the constriction, and already it had prevented her from eating and drinking water: and she had almost reached death, if one day or two days had remained.
And the abscess had risen so much that it sent out two branches, until they came out above the two nasal passages.
I hurried quickly, and I fixed a hook into one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large part was drawn out from it.
Then I cut it off where it protruded from the nasal opening.
Then I did the same to the one that protruded from the other nasal opening.
Then I opened her mouth and pressed her tongue: afterwards I fixed a hook into the abscess: then I cut off part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it: thus the woman's throat was healed, and she began to drink water immediately, then took some food.
Therefore, I did not cease to cut from that abscess many times over a long period: and the abscess would grow back in place of what I had cut, until it became excessively prolonged with it.
I then devised a plan and cauterized the abscess in the throat.
It therefore ceased from growing: then I left her side, and I did not know what God did with her after me.
On the incision of the apostematis of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula, and it becomes apostematous, and is long: then it is called perpendicular. And if it is thick, round, in the lower part, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help: and you see that the warm apostem has settled from it, and is thin: then you must cut it.
And what is gathered from it is round, and does not have length, and is bloody, or of a brown color, or black, and does not have feeling: then you must avoid cutting it. For in it there is fear for the patient.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner I have described by its whiteness and length, you should make the patient sit before the sun: and press his tongue with the instrument of which the description preceded: then fix a hook in the uvula, and pull it down: and cut it with one of the two instruments I mentioned for incising both tonsils.
And you must not cut from it anything except what extends beyond its natural limit without additional tissue.
For if you cut more from it, you will damage the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use what we have mentioned in the incision of both tonsils: and treat it until it heals.
If however the patient is terrified of the incision: then it is necessary that you use ingenuity in his cauterization without fear and dread.
And the method of the cautery on it is not to be done except with sharp water: and it is that you place the patient's head in your lap. Then press his tongue with the instrument we have mentioned. Then take from the water that I told you about in the chapter on cauterizing, and mix it with unextinguished lime, and make it neither thick nor thin, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, which has this form.
Let the end of it where the medicine is placed be concave, like the concavity of a spoon's bowl.
And place the instrument with the medicine on the ulcer itself: and let the patient lie on his side, so that saliva runs out of his mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing descends from it to his throat and injures him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you should apply it to the ulcer for a quantity of half an hour, until you see it already blackened, and the biting effect of the medicine calmed.
And if you want to take a cloth, and wrap it over the end of the probe, and pour the cloth with medicine, and insert the probe with the cloth into the tube above, until the cloth adheres to the ulcer.
Do this many times, until you achieve what you desire from the cauterization of the ulcer. Then leave it: for it dries up and falls off after three days, or four.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after cauterization, clean around with a grape soaked in butter, and cleanse with it what is around it from the medicine: and mix it with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
This method of treatment, therefore, is more healthful than incision and farther from danger.
And sometimes it is cured with something finer than cautery and incision: for it is cured with fumigation, according to this method. Take calamus, hyssop, origanum, ruta, sseah, chamomile, abrotanum, and similar herbs: and gather all of them or some of them in a pot, and let them be submerged with vinegar, and boiled: and the pot should be sealed with clay properly: and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot over which a concave instrument should be placed according to this method.
