Then I examined all the openings: and I found them to connect with each other from all parts of the leg.
I hurried and cut above one of those openings until I exposed part of the bone: and I found it corrupted: already eaten away, blackened, rotten, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
I cut away what was revealed to me and prepared from the corrupted bone.
And I ensured that there was nothing in the bone except that corruption which I cut off and sawed away. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I placed the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines for a long period. And it was not consolidated.
Then I probed and exposed above the bone a second time beyond the first exposure: and I found the corruption continuing with the bone: and I sawed away what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
I exposed it again and did not cease to cut the bone piece by piece, and attempted its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut about one palm's length from the bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored him with medicines, and it was consolidated quickly, and healed: and this repetition was not necessary in his operation, and his cutting, unless because of the disposition of the patient's weakness, and the scarcity of his tolerance, and my fear for him of death: for it happened to him at all hours from the excess of evacuation evil faintings.
He was therefore healed with a complete healing: and hard flesh was born in place of the bone, and his condition was made good in his body, and his strength returned: and he moved about in his conditions: and walking did not harm him at all.
If however a prominent bone in an already fractured part of the body has broken: then it is necessary to suture it, according to this method: and it is as follows: take a ligature, and tie it at the end of the protruding bone: and command someone to pull it upwards: and place another thicker ligature made of wool beside the first ligature. Then bind it over the flesh that is below the bone: and extend its end so that you pull the flesh downwards: and you cover the flesh over the place you wish to saw, so that the saw does not harm the flesh.
Place wood or a board under the lower bone appropriately: for when you do this, it does not prevent cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary for the sawing to be over the corrupted area temporarily, so that there is no corruption in the concavity of the bone: and it does not appear to the sense in its appearance, and compels another to take its place in sawing.
If the bone is corrupted and is not prominent, but rather its part is continuous with another part: and the corruption is in the middle of it or in a part of it: then uncover the flesh from all sides entirely. Then place the wood below.
Then saw it from the first side where there is corruption until the sawing continues from the other side: and let the sawing be according to the length from the corruption for a little while, as we have said.
If the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it continues from the other side.
If however the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no remedy but scraping.
If the corruption is in the carpal bones of the hand or in the tarsal bones of the foot: then the matter is very difficult. But it is necessary that you inquire how the corruption appears to you. And scrape it, and clean it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever method is corrected for you: when the Vein or Nerve does not oppose you.
And know that the cutting instruments and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and their fineness, and their size, and their smallness, and their hardness, and their rarity. Therefore, it is necessary that you prepare every kind of instrument suitable for that operation.
And know that these operations indicate to you the kind of instrument you need when you have thorough study with you. And the knowledge of the methods of this art is the testimony of the methods of illnesses: then you will find by yourself that which is suitable for it from the instruments for every sickness.
And I am confirming for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will place on examples over which you will cut, and the test by which you will experiment on others.

And it is not suitable unless the heads of the joints are scraped with it when they are corrupted, or if the mouth is wide and large.

This resembles a file, and the end is like a saw: it is used in many places for the reason of bones.
The form of a shaving that is suitable for the purpose of what is perforated in bones.
Its end is triangular, with sharp ends.
It is made from India. And similarly, all the things we have mentioned should be made from razors, and incisors, and saws.
About the incision of extremities, and theSerratura ossium
Extremities putrefy, either from an external cause or an internal cause.
Therefore, when you have treated that corruption with medical treatment and the cure does not help, and you see the corruption spreading to the limb, which nothing can stop: then it is necessary to amputate that limb until where the corruption follows, so that the patient may be saved from death or a worse malice than the loss of the limb.
And the sign of one to whom that happens is that the limb blackens until it rots: whether the fire burned it, or it rotted after the blackening: until that rot is cut into what follows that limb, and creeps throughout the entire body.
Hasten therefore with the incision of it, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of some venomous creatures: such as the sea scorpion, and viper, and centipede, and similar ones.
If therefore the corruption or sting is at the extremity of a finger, then cut off the finger, and do not give space to the corruption so that it may spread to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut it off from the root of the wrist, and do not give space to the corruption until it takes hold of the arm's wrist.
And if it invades it, then cut the arm at the elbow joint.
If however the corruption spreads, and you see it around the elbow: then there is death of the patient in that case. And to administer beyond that in the treatment is an excess of potency.
And similarly do for the foot when the corruption invades a toe, then cut it at one of the joints: and if it takes hold of the arch of the foot, then amputate the entire foot. If however it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the joint of the knee.
If the corruption has already ascended above the knee, then there is no remedy for it except amputation, and leaving the patient to death.
And the method of cutting a limb or sawing it is to tie the lower ligament where you wish to cut; and tie another ligament above the place. And the minister extends one ligament downward; and the minister extends another ligament upward. And you separate the flesh which is between the two ligaments with a broad spatula until all the flesh is exposed. Then it shall be cut or sawed.
And it is necessary to place linen cloth on all sides, and the saw should not touch the healthy area: and that an additional injury and a hot abscess do not occur to the patient.
If internal bleeding occurs while you are operating, then cauterize the area quickly: or apply certain powders that stop the flow of blood. Then return to your treatment until it is complete.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and care for it until it is healed.
And I tell you what happened to a man in his foot, the same incident that I am telling you about.
Which is that a blackening occurred in his foot, with a burning resembling the combustion of fire: and that corruption first occurred in his toe, until it began to affect the entire foot.
Therefore, the man hurried to act on his own after he saw corruption setting in with severe pain in the limb, which he discovered due to the pain and burning: and he cut it at the joint, and was healed.
And after a long time had passed, that same corruption occurred in his index finger.
He came to me, and I tried to recover that excess by applying medicine to his hand after cleansing his body: but the excess did not subside, and it began to spread to another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
He called upon me to amputate his hand: and I refused to do so, hoping to address that excess by myself: and I also feared his death from the incision of his hand. For the virtue of the man was at stake.
After he despaired of me, he returned to his land: and news reached me that he hurried and amputated his entire hand: and he was healed.
And I have not brought this account unless to be of help for that which falls under this kind of illness: and to provide guidance by which it may be understood, and according to which action may be taken.
Regarding the treatment of sinuses, and the nature of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy members and the space of the abscess extends until it forms a collection of pus: then it bursts or is perforated, and what is inside comes out as pus, and a vacant place remains like a vessel: and the skin that is above it is like a cloth that has become thin: and it does not progress to its ultimate corruption, nor does it affect the mouth, nor the nerve, nor the ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus: and it is not named a Fistula: however, if its space extends until the corruption affects any of these members, then it is named a Fistula or syke.
And the care is to cut that skin entirely as it rolls up: and especially when it becomes thin, and made like cloth: and you trust that it does not adhere to the place, because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin adheres, and does not result from that final corruption: and if there is thickness in the skin from flesh, then care for it with aclyster: and it is to consider towards the sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus emitted from it has a foul odor: then cleanse it with Egyptian medicine, which is of vinegar, oil, and ziniar, equal parts received, gathered in a vessel: and decocted over fire, until the medicine turns red: and comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take what you need from it: and dissolve it with water and honey, and cleanse the sinus with it: and bind its opening, and leave the medicine in it for a duration of two hours.
Then extract it with pressing: do this for as many days until the sinus is cleansed and the odor is removed.
And sometimes it is cleansed with ashes water when this medicine is not available to you: and it is to take the ash of vine wood, or ash from holm oak wood: and pour water over it. Then boil it, and cleanse with it until you trust that the sinus is washed clean.
If the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine or ashes water, then cleanse it with water and honey, to which some ground ziniar is already mixed. Or cleanse it with honey and wine mixed together, for it is the property of honey to purify and wash clean: and wine causes adhesion, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you do this several times and are confident that the sinus corruption has been removed, then cleanse it with something that promotes flesh growth.
And it is as follows: take some from the dyafinicon plaster and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or cleanse it with certain medicines and other ointments whose properties I have written about in the treatise on ointments.
If the sinus opening is narrow, through which a clyster tube cannot fit: then enlarge it temporarily with iron; or place lint wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment on it until it is enlarged.
And similarly, if the opening is also wide, then bring the edges together with a suture: and leave enough space for the clyster to enter without added obstruction.
And similarly, if the opening from which pus runs is elevated towards the upper parts: then cut it at a lower place within it so that pus may run down from it: for when pus is confined in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from generating in it.
If it is not possible for you to cut the sinus around its lower part as you wish: then try to prepare the area so that pus may run out easily according to how it is prepared for you. Strive so that no pus at all remains in it: and take the binding medicines which are placed on the wound, suitable for what you wish. And spread them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that it covers the entire sinus. Then cut with the scissors directly at the opening of the sinus hole, slightly larger than the opening of the sinus. Then spread the mild ointments over another cloth according to the size of the hole: and place it on it at a distance from the opening of the sinus, so that the exit of pus from it is alleviated: and do not remove the large cloth unless after several days one by one.
The one who is on the opening of the sinus is the one whom you remove every hour, so that what accumulates in it from pus may come out: and to know whether what runs out of it as a virus is much or little: or digested or undigested.
And with this, examine again the site of the sinus itself, whether its owner feels pain in it or there is no pain in it, and whether there is a swelling in it or there is no swelling in it.
When therefore your treatment is extended thus, and you see the matter that comes out over the opening of the sinus, then you know that the sinus has adhered or is approaching adhesion: then dissolve it in the morning or on the third day: and when you dissolve the wound, then change to another cloth and renew the ointment.
If however it exits from the sinus after a long interval a fine pus, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, since there are those among men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and among them are those who act contrary to that.
If however it exits from the sinus after many days as undigested pus, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If therefore the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its condition is prolonged: then add to the drying of medicines, but let the physicians make the medicines moist in their substance and dry in their effect: such as diafinicon ointment, when an excess addition of cumin is added to it.
And from the very last method of treatment, it is to take diafinicon ointment, and dissolve it with rose oil, and spray over it equal quality old wine in its substance.
Then bind it with it, and use it.
And now it does a similar action to this when you do not have diafinicon ointment present, honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense: grind all of it, and powder it over honey while it is over the fire. Then apply it on the cloth, and bind it over the sinus: and apply with honey which you boiled until it thickened: and powder over it the spices, and place it on the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes administered in that is aristolochia longa, and roots of lily celestial, and it is lily: and flour of herb, and centaury. Use these simples, or combined, grind them, and sieve them: and powder over the honey which I mentioned, and use it.
If the condition of the Sinus is prolonged and not healed by this treatment: then know that there is corruption deep within it: or it has already made an impression on the bones, or on other hard members, or ligaments: then treat it with the treatment for Fistulas, according to what has been mentioned.
Concerning the treatment of panaritium and ingrown nails, and the incision of the added finger, and amputation.
Panaritium is an abscess that arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot: and it may also arise in the other digits.
When therefore the condition is prolonged and neglected by its healer, and it becomes abscessed with a warm abscess: or it corrupts and emits pus until it corrodes the root of the nail: and it may corrupt it entirely, and the corruption may reach the bone, until there is a foul odor: and the extremity of the digit becomes swollen, and its color becomes dark.
When therefore you treat it with what we said in the division, and your treatment does not heal it: then you must cut with iron all the excess that remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cautery is very helpful.
If the bone is healthy and the nail is also healthy, and the growths of the nail already press on the flesh towards the inside, and begin to pierce it, and harm it: then you must place a fine wedge under the angle of the nail that pierces the flesh, and push it upwards: and cut it with ease, and apply over what remains of the flesh medicines that burn, corrosives, until all of it is removed. Then care for it with ointments until it heals.
If the corruption has already set into the bone, then you must cut that bone and remove it: for it will never heal as long as the corrupted bone remains.
If you see corrosion and corruption spreading to the finger, then cut it at one of the joints, according to what has been previously mentioned. Then care for it until it heals.
If the nail is subjected to abrasion or impact, and severe pain ensues, then you must bleed the patient first. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatumilia in a horizontal section from top to view.
And be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause severe pain to the patient by doing so, and it will result in additional flesh growing in that place.
Then treat the area with something that relieves pain.
The additional finger that is born on some men's hands is perhaps entirely fleshy: and perhaps there are bones in some of them, and perhaps there is a nail in it: and some of them are born at the root of the joint, or its birth is in some knots of the finger. He who is born on some knots of the finger does not move: and he who is born near the joint of the finger, perhaps moves.
The cutting of the one who is fleshy among them is easy. Which means you should cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
The treatment of the one whose birth is at the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore, abstain from cutting it.
In the one who is born on the finger near the last knot, then you must cut through its flesh first with a circular incision down to the bone. Then file the bone with one of those files suitable for it. Then care for it until it heals.
The joining that occurs between fingers often happens either from the birth of the person, that is, it is born with him, or from the healing of a wound, or from burning by fire, and similar causes.
Therefore, you must cut that joining until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them linen, or cloths soaked in rose oil: so that they do not consolidate too quickly, and separate between them: or place between them a thin sheet of lead until the area consolidates as it should.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs between certain fingers and the palm, then cut that consolidation according to how it is prepared and suits the shape of the member.
Regarding the Incision of Vitium.
Uites are convoluted veins, thick, full of melancholic superfluities which occur in many parts of the body: and they most frequently appear in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers and workers, and those who carry loads.
Therefore, you must administer body shaking from black bile several times with strong shaking.
Then bleed the patient from the brachial vein.
The treatment of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is to cut them and extract the black blood. And the other method is to elevate the vein and remove it entirely.
The incision of it is according to this method. Soak the leg first with hot water extensively until the thick, feculent blood is dissolved. Then bind the leg of the patient from the upper part of the thigh down to the lower part of the knee with a strap. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then draw, that is, extract the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg up to the top, and it is necessary for the phlebotomist to bleed the patient from the upper part first. Then cut the nail all the way down: until a quantity of blood emerges that you judge sufficient and that the patient's strength can tolerate.
Then bind it: and instruct him to abstain from foods that generate black bile: and repeat the evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are full, and if it harms the patient.
The extraction of it is according to this method. Shave the leg of the patient if there are many hairs on it. Then introduce him to the bath: or foment his leg with warm water until it reddens and the veins swell: or exercise him with strenuous exercise, if a bath is not available, until the limb warms up.
Then cut the skin directly along the vein's length: either at its end near the knee, or at the lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and scrape the vein from all sides until it is visible to the sense: and it appears when you see it turning from red to black. When it emerges from the skin, you will see it white like a string.
Then introduce a probe under it until when it is raised and emerges from the skin: suspend it with a blind hook, gently.
Then cut another section near that section with three fingers. Then remove the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then raise it with probes as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then cut another section, or multiple sections, if you need them. Then raise it, and cut it with the final section near the heel.
Then pull it to the section which is above it.
And do this until you pull it out from the upper section, or higher than all the sections, until when it comes out entirely: then cut it.
If it does not obey you to be pulled out and raised: then introduce a needle with a strong double thread, and tie it, and pull it out: or introduce a probe under it, and rotate your hand in all directions until it comes out. And be careful that it is not cut. For if it is cut, it becomes very difficult for you to raise it, and great harm comes to the patient from it.
Therefore, when you have pulled it out entirely: then place wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, on the wound site: and care for it until it heals.
If the vein is contracted, having a twisting which coils towards the sides, and it is not straight as we said: then you must incise over it on all parts of its twisting, and at the places of its coiling.
Then suspend it with hooks until you raise it entirely: and be careful at your cut over it that you do not cut the vein or injure it: for it will be difficult for you to raise or extract it. Therefore, keep your caution.
Form of an incident in which a vein is healed.
Form of a blind hook.
It does not have a curve like other hooks, nor does it have a sharp end to avoid injuring the vein: and it has a thick, flexible shape, gentle: because if it were thin, it would cut the vein with its thinness: rather, let it have excessive thickness, as we said.
On the extraction of a vein from the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in warm lands, such as Al-Andalus, and the land of Arabs, and in dry, warm lands, lacking moisture, that is, where no herb or tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from the putrefaction which occurs under the skin, like the occurrence of snakes and ascaris worms within bodies, and worms generated between the skin and the flesh.
And the sign of the onset of this vein's event is that vehement inflammation occurs in the leg. Then the place is cut open. Then the vein begins to emerge from that place of incision, as if it were the root of a plant or an animal.
When therefore its end appears from it, then you must wrap a fragment of lead over it, whose weight is from one fourth of a drachm to two drachms, and tie it to the knot itself: and leave the lead hanging down from the leg.
Whenever anything emerges from it to the outer part, wrap it in lead, and tie it up. If it extends too much, then cut part of it off, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root before it comes out entirely. For if you cut it, it contracts and enters the flesh: and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the area, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore, it must be handled gently and extracted gradually until it comes out entirely from its end, and nothing of it remains in the body: and from this vein in some men comes out something that is five palms and ten long. And it has come to my attention that it came out of a man who was twenty palms long.
If it happens to be cut during your treatment: then insert a probe into its opening, and pierce it with a long hole, along with the body, until all the material inside is evacuated: and treat the putrefaction of the area with medicines for several days. Then treat the abscesses themselves.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the ankle joint, or on the foot itself: and it has multiple openings, and a branch emerges from every opening.
Treat it therefore as we have said in the division, and along with what has been mentioned.
Regarding the incision for worms generated under the skin, and it is called Bouina disease.
This disease is called Bouina disease in some regions among us because it often affects oxen, and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it moves throughout the entire body, ascending and descending: and its presence is noticed during its movement from one limb to another, until it breaks through the skin wherever it emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. Like worms and serpents, that is, earthworms, and roundworms in the stomach.
And it is feared because of its maliciousness. Because when it moves within the body and rises to the head, and reaches a place: then it may open up there and emerge, and destroy the eye: and this often happens.
Therefore, when you wish to treat it and extract it, then it can only be done during its movement and when its presence is noticeable.
Therefore, you must bind what is above him and what is below him with a strong binding ligature. Then cut above it, and remove it.
If it is deep in the flesh and you do not find it, then place a hot cautery on the spot until it dies.
And what is more feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If you see it has already reached the head near the eye, then bind below it the eyebrow with a good constriction. Then cut above it, and remove it.
And it is necessary for the patient to purify his body with medicines that dissolve foul putrid humors.
And let him beware of foods that cause putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the disease Nakir.
This disease is called Nakir in our land: it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, then shifts from one limb to another.
And indeed, I have already seen it, as I tell you. I was summoned to a sick woman in certain villas. And she exposed her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the rope-like vein of the arm.
And after I remained for one hour, I saw one inflation proceed with a bladder, like a worm proceeds, ascending to his shoulder faster than it can be: so that it is like quicksilver, when it runs from place to place.
Therefore, the pain departed from that place and fixed on the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran through the rest of the body until it reached the other arm.
Then he told me that it was moving throughout his whole body, as I saw.
I marveled at the speed of its transformation from one member to another. And indeed, before that, I had not seen this sickness with my own eyes in such a manner according to this way. However, I have seen many who find the pain change from one member to another; and I did not see it with my eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it to be anything except that the woman was one of those who have dry bodies and exposed veins; hence this wind-changing appears to the senses.
And it is necessary that they do not appear in this manner in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with hidden veins.
Therefore, when you wish for its treatment and the patient feels that pain: then if it is apparent to your eye as we said, then bind above it and below it quickly: and cut above it until the confined wind exits: and cauterize the place.
If however you do not see it with your own eyes: then treat it with shaking of the body, and with that which expels wind, and soothes it with foul pills, and pills of fennel, and similar.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows vary according to their types, and according to the places into which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is as follows: some have large tips, and small ones: and concave, and deaf. And among them are those which have three angles. And among them are those which have tongues. And among them are those which have barbs.
Those, however, which fall according to the limbs into which they fall, are in two ways. For either the limbs are concave principal limbs, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and those similar to these. When therefore it falls into any of these limbs, and signs of death, which I tell you afterward, appear to you, then you must avoid extracting that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in most cases. And when those bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the limb, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it and penetrates the bone, and exits through the siphac which is above the brain, are: for there occur from that severe pain, and blindness, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasms, and confusion of the mind, and vomiting of bile. And perhaps blood comes out from the nostrils, or the ears. And perhaps speech is impaired, and the voice is destroyed. And there comes out from the place of the wound a white moisture similar to pus. And there exits from it something similar to a watery state of the flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not exit.
The signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left nipple: and one feels with it as if it were fixed in a hard substance, not in an empty space. And perhaps the arrow has a movement similar to the movement of the pulse: and black blood runs from the wound. And following that is the coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore that death undoubtedly approaches.
And the sign of the arrow when it wounds the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound: and the vessels that follow the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes. And he exhales a great, deep breath: and seeks the attraction of cold air.
If the arrow falls into the membrane that is in the chest: then it is near the ribs: and a great breath with intense pain, with a sigh, occurs: and all the shoulder limbs move.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from the food exits from the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the abdomen and is fixed in it: something of feces exits from the wound, or a tumor, or an intestine that is already torn. Therefore, there is no hope for his cure, nor for the extraction of the arrow.
If the arrow falls into the bladder and urine exits: and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger on the patient: then you know that it is mortal.
The other members, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and helper, and spine of the back, and collarbone, and thigh, and shin, and those similar parts are treated according to many arrangements: when the arrow does not find an artery or a nerve: and the arrow is not venomous.
And I tell you what I have seen regarding these arrows, so that you may apply it to your treatment.
This is because an arrow fell into a man's lacrimal of his eye, at the root of his nose. I extracted it from her other side, under the fat of her ear: and she was healed, and no harm came to her eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which fell into the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I could only reach a small part of it with which it adhered to its wood: and it was a large arrow among the arrows of Turkish bows, with soft iron, in which there are no ears.
The Jew was healed, and no harmful accident happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is the one that has barbs. I sewed over it between two fingers: and it was already submerged in his throat. I carefully extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his abdomen: and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed and there was no change in his condition, I sewed over the arrow: and I operated on it, and extracted it: and he was healed, and no harmful accident happened to him.
And I saw a man to whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound had healed over it. After seven years had passed, an arrow emerged from the root of his hip bone.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow fell into her abdomen, and the wound healed over it, and the arrow remained inside her; and none of her conditions were changed, and the woman did not find any harm from it in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow fell on his face: and the wound healed, and it remained, not causing him much pain: and there are many similar cases to these.
And I extracted an arrow from a man who was one of the King's leaders, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and it had already deviated slightly to the right side: and the entire arrow was hidden.
I was called therefore for his treatment after the arrow fell for up to three days. I found therefore the arrow wound very tight.
I examined it therefore with a fine probe, and did not feel it: and he indeed felt a puncture and pain near his right ear. I suspected therefore that it was that puncture from the tip of the arrow.
I treated the area with a plaster that had attracting and dissolving properties, confident that the area would form an abscess, and reveal to me a sign of the arrow, so I could cut above it: and nothing happened in the area indicating that the arrow had reached its location.
I therefore applied a plaster to it for many days: and no accident occurred, and the wound was healed in the meantime: and he remained weak from its extraction for a space of days, until he felt the arrow one day inside his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. I therefore applied a sharp and corrosive medicine to that wound for many days until it was open: and when I probed it, I felt the fine tip of the arrowhead adhering to the wood.
Then I applied that sharp medicine to the opening of the wound until the tip of the arrow became visibly apparent: and I spent with him a period of time around four months.
Then after the wound had enlarged, and it became possible for me to insert forceps into it and move it, and it did not obey to come out: then I did not cease to refine and devise methods of ingenuity on it, until one day I began to work on it with properly made forceps, according to how their form will appear at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed the physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose does not restore itself. I therefore restored it, and the wound was healed, and the sick person was made whole in health: and nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you the nature of the extraction of certain arrows: that you may set it as a rule and meaning on what I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with narration: nor does the book comprehend it, and the wise artisan compares little with much: and that which is present over that which is absent: and finds a new operation and a new instrument among the descents of foreign things of this art when they occur.
I say therefore, that arrows do not depart from the limbs in which they are fixed, except in two ways. Or by attraction from the place from which it entered. Or by the opposite part of another.
And that which exits where it entered is an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place: and it is attracted and exits.
If it does not obey exiting from the time it falls: then you must leave it for several days until the flesh around it putrefies: and its attraction is lessened, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in the bone and does not obey you to exit, then leave it for several days: and repeat it with attraction and motion every day until it exits.
If it does not obey you to exit after days, then you must drill around the arrow in the bone itself on all sides with a fine drill until the area around the arrow is enlarged. Then attract it, and extract it.
If however the arrow is already hidden in a part of the body and is concealed and hidden from perception: then seek it with a probe. If however you feel it, then remove it with some instruments which are suitable for its removal.
If however you cannot reach it because of the constriction of the wound and the length of the arrow embedded deeply: and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then incise over it until the wound enlarges, and it becomes possible to extract the arrow.
If it has ears by which it can be held, then free the flesh fixed to it on all sides with every means you can employ, and use methods if it cannot be freed from the flesh by separating the ears and twisting them until it comes out.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps to all sides, until you make it come out: and be very careful to avoid breaking the arrow, so that its removal does not become difficult for you.
If you cannot do it within one hour, then leave it for several days until what is around it putrefies. Then repeat it: for the skin will heal. If however the flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we said about the treatment in its chapter: and guard your skill from cutting the vein, or nerve, or chord.
And apply your skill in every way possible to avoid the Arrow: and do it with ease, tranquility, slowness, and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary that you do when you pull out the Arrow, that the patient be positioned according to the shape in which he was at the time of the Arrow's impact. For it is more beneficial. If however it is not possible for you to do that, then administer what is possible for you from the positions.
But the Arrow which exits from the opposite side of the other part, or already protrudes somewhat to the exterior, or causes the tip of the Arrow to bulge through the sensation from the upper part of the nearby skin, and you see it prominent: cut above it, and let the incision be according to the amount that forceps can grasp in it.
Then pull it out: for it becomes easy to remove.
If however it is lodged in the bone, then rotate your hand according to the roundness, until the Arrow impresses on the bone and enlarges its own space: then pull it out.
And if not: leave it for several days: then try again until it comes out.
If the arrow shaft is in it, then push it with it.
If the shaft has fallen off and you wish to administer thrusting: then introduce a concave instrument to it, so that its concavity enters into the tail of the arrow: then push it with it.
If the arrow is concave, then push it with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow is alleviated by that.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to make concave all the flesh where the poison is: if it is possible for you to do so. Then treat it with whatever is appropriate for that.
If the arrow falls in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side: and it is close to what you touch with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and be careful of cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it.
Then sew the wound, if you see it, and treat it until it heals.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the ends be similar to a bird's beak, which are made like blades: when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And many types are made from them, large, small, and medium, according to the quantity of the size of the Arrow, and its smallness, and the width of the wound, and its narrowing.
Form of the Concave Force.
And the form of the force of the deaf.
This is the end of the deaf as with the radius, so that its entry into the concave arrow is alleviated, and it is propelled with it.
About the bloodletting of veins.
The veins that are accustomed to be bloodletted in the body are thirty.
Of these, in the head there are sixteen: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbae: and two arteries, which appear on both temples: and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called Aspicientes: and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead: and the vein which is at the end of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
The veins which are bled in the arms are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalica, and it is on the outer side: and the common people call it the head vein. And the Fusca. And it is the Median vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalica: and the common people call it the body vein. And the Basilica, and it is placed on the inner side: and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the Funis brachii, and it is placed above the bone: and it is the one in which bleeding is performed, and it is the one which appears above the thumb with clear manifestation.
And the Saluatella: and it is the vein which is between the ring finger and the little finger: and it has two branches.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is the one which is under the curvature of the thumb on the outer side. And the second is the Saphena: and its location is near the heel on the inner side. And the vein Ness: and its location is at the heel on the woodland side.
And in the other leg there are three similar veins.
On the bloodletting of two veins behind the ears.
The bloodletting of the two veins which are behind the ears is beneficial for old catarrhs, hemiplegia, sahafati, and old bad ulcers of the head.
And the quality of bloodletting it is according to what I narrate. And it is, that the head of the patient is shaved: and his occipital area at the site of the two veins is rubbed with a coarse cloth by good friction. Then the patient should constrict his neck with a cloth until two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears at two depressed areas of the head. Seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark that spot with encaustic.
Then take the knife scalpel, and it is called the Incider. Then insert it under the vein into the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut the vein with the skin by an intersecting incision. And the length of the incision should be the width of two joined fingers, or about that: and let as much blood flow as you wish.
Then bind it with poultices, and leave it until it heals.
And already at the beginning of the book, the incision of them and their cauterization have preceded.
The benefit of bloodletting the two arteries which are in the two temples is for ancient hemiplegia: and for false sodoma, and persistent ophthalmia: and for the course of sharp humors flowing to the eyes.
And the method of bloodletting them is as I tell you. Let the patient constrict his neck greatly until two veins appear clearly to the sight: and their pulse is made evident under your finger. Then mark them with cauterization.
Then lift the skin over the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the lancet piercing from below, and lift the vein upwards. And cut it as you did with the other two veins: and let the blood flow according to the amount necessary for your case.
Then release the patient's constriction, and place your finger on the vein for one hour. Then apply a bandage and poultices on it, and bind it tightly with a strong constriction: and leave it until it heals.
And already at the beginning of the book, the mention of them, and their incision, and their extraction have preceded.
The benefit of bleeding the frontal vein after bleeding the Cephalic is for old ailments of the face: and for hemigrana, and redness: and ugly sores.
And the quality of its bleeding is as I tell you. The patient should constrict his neck with a ligature until the vein appears. Then take the instrument which is called the Fossorium.
Place the sharp point which is on the head of the Fossorium directly on the vein, and strike over it with a comb or with another instrument in its manner; and let the blood flow in the quantity you desire. Then release the ligature of the patient and press it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad lancet. However, it should not have a sharp end like other lancets, but rather a broad end. And bleed it transversely because the mouth is nearby, and the lancet might break there when it is thin.
The benefit of the two veins which are in the two tear ducts of both eyes is for the eye from scabies and redness: and film in the eye, and ailments of the face.
And the quality of their bloodletting is that the sick person should constrict his neck with a strap. Then bloodlet them: and you shall stand over his head.
And the bloodletting should be done across the length slightly with a small, somewhat broad bloodletter, for there is no flesh in that place. For if the bloodletter has a delicate tip, it may break.
Then let as much blood flow as you need. And place a cloth over them, bind them for one night, and then loosen them.
The bloodletting of the nasal veins is beneficial for acute fever, severe headache, and facial ailments such as hemiplegia, and the redness that occurs on the face, especially when it is chronic.
And the quality of its bloodletting is that the sick person should constrict his neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a fine, long bloodletter: and insert it in the middle of the nasal septum, between the parts that divide the nose vertically. Because the vein does not appear there to the senses, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
And you should keep your hand with the bloodletter for a moment: and let the blood flow according to your need. Then bind it for one night. For it is restored quickly.
The benefit of the two Guidege bloodlettings is for constriction of breathing, and the onset of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and bad sores, and corrosives, that is, serpent-like herpes.
And the quality of their bloodletting is such that the patient should bind himself under both sides of his neck with a bandage: and the practitioner should stand above the head of the patient, and the patient should be sitting on a seat.
Then the bloodletter should cut the vein to the length of a broad incision temporarily. Then draw from the blood a moderate quantity, or according to what you see is necessary for that. Then do likewise with the other vein.
Then remove the bandage, and bind the two veins with a moderate constriction: so that the patient is not choked. And leave it until morning. For the wound is healed.
On the bloodletting of veins by Algeberich. The benefit of Algeberich's bloodletting is that it helps with alcohol in the mouth, and corruption of the gums: and bad sores: and cracks in the lips, and bad ulcers, even those that are in the nose, and around it.
And the quality of their bloodletting is that you make the sick person sit before you: and hold his neck with a rope. Then turn his lip back, and look at the two veins: one is on the right side of the lip, and the second on the left side of it: and the indication of both is by the blackness of both. And that is true: because around them are small veins, black. Cut therefore both with a penetrating incision.
If however there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is: then aim at the incision of the larger of them, and the more apparent of them.
And similarly do with the two veins which are in the upper lip: and indeed the custom greatly agrees with this: and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Regarding bloodletting of the vein under the tongue after cephalic bloodletting. The benefit of bloodletting the two veins which are under the tongue is that cephalic bloodletting is for obstructions which are in the throat, and sickness of the uvula, and diseases of the mouth.
And the method of bloodletting them is that the sick person should sit before you in the sunlight: and lift his tongue, and look under the tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for another vein: and the color of both tends towards blackness.
Bloodlet therefore from both: and be careful not to hesitate in bloodletting them. For under it are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood may occur from those arteries.
The three veins which are bled at the elbow are those in whose bleeding a custom has run through all people.
And their bloodletting is done in two ways. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle lancet: or sharpening to a fineness: or cutting with a knife-like lancet, and it is called Alnesil. And this is its form.
And among them are broad and fine varieties according to the width of the veins and their tightness. The form of the myrtle lancet is broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave veins, full veins, protruding veins, visible veins, thick veins, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this lancet is of lesser breadth and finer at the tip.
And it is suitable for bloodletting of fine veins that contain fine yellow blood.
However, the Basilica vein, which is one of these three veins, is beneficial for bloodletting. Because it extracts blood from illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from that which follows the chest, and the belly: and it is fitting for the bloodletter to be cautious with it, to beware, and be in fear of it, for an artery is beneath it. If therefore he errs and adds to the bloodletting puncture, he will cut that artery, and a flow of blood will occur. Therefore, it is necessary that the bloodletter should not puncture it with the bloodletting needle; rather, it should be cut with a scalpel.
If the basilic vein does not appear with a clear manifestation, then it is necessary to avoid it and switch to another, or seek some branch of it, or bloodlet at the forearm instead. For it is evident, and cut it with the bloodletting scalpel, as we have said.
If you wish to bloodlet from it, then it is necessary before constricting the arm, to touch the area until you recognize the pulse location. Then mark the spot with ink, afterwards bind the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using the scalpel, as we have said. And be careful that the incision does not fall away from the location of the artery.
And when you see, as you tighten the band, swelling at the place you marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of an artery: avoid it therefore.
If you see the blood at the bloodletting spurt like a boy's urine, and it is fine and red, then you know that it is from an artery. Then therefore hurry, and place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger. If therefore the blood is cut off (for it is often cut off), then bind the arm, and release it: and let the patient avoid moving it: and let it be under fear.
And let him not move it for several days until it is healed.
If however the blood is not cut off, and overpowers you: and your medicine is not present at your moment: then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities contract, and the blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of fig, and dry it: take one half, and bind it over the place of the vein's constriction properly, with a bandage and poultices until the next day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not: then treat it with that which precedes the memory from the place of powders cutting off the flow of blood: and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in most conditions, because of the small place of the wound, and the possibility of binding from the arm.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the Fusca vein is that it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body: therefore, because it is composed of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Cephalica, as we have said.
And it is fitting for the bloodletter to do so, that she be above fear from her bloodletting, for under it is a nerve. If therefore he adds to the bloodletting puncture and finds a nerve, stupor occurs in it, and its healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not healed thoroughly.
And this nerve often appears to the sense. If however it is hidden in some men, and is subtle, and does not show: then you must place your bloodletting where you bloodlet, a cut with Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If a vein is between two nerves, then find the vein along its length.
Regarding the cephalic vein. Bloodletting from the cephalic vein is beneficial because it draws blood from the head and contributes to illnesses of the eyes.
And it is proper in this vein, if you wish, to bloodlet it by piercing with an olive lancet, or with a myrtle lancet: for it is safer than all veins, for there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
However, it is fitting for you in bloodletting to avoid the armpit with the lancet and seek a softer spot: and it does not harm if it is not found on the first attempt, so that it may be repeated upon it several times with bloodletting.
However, it may become abscessed in some men when it is not bloodlet from on the first attempt, but that abscess does not harm it significantly.
On the usefulness of bloodletting, and the precautions to be observed in it. The quality of bloodletting, and its consequences depend on it, you must consider in its correction.
Therefore, the first thing you should know is that bloodletting is not administered except for the preservation of health, its persistence, and caution against the onset of illnesses.
And let bloodletting be performed on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Basilic, and the Cubital. And let bloodletting be performed at the beginning of spring on an empty stomach, in spring and summer: and in autumn and winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And let bloodletting be performed on the first day, or on the third, after three hours have passed since daybreak.
Bloodletting which is administered in illnesses does not have a fixed time: but when convenience and necessity call for it, whether at night or day, and at any hour, and at any time.
And it should not be that children be bled until they have passed fourteen years. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth month up to the seventh. Nor should seniors who have already passed sixty years be bled.
Therefore, when someone wishes to be bled for any reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their intestines before bloodletting with a mildclyster if there is much retained stool in them: so that the veins do not attract putrid and harmful superfluities from the intestines during bloodletting to the principal members.
The patient should not be bled when he is completely sated, that is, when he has nausea-inducing fullness; nor when he is drunk, nor as he recovers from drunkenness, until those conditions are removed from them. Nor the patient with dropsy.
And caution should be taken not to perform bloodletting again after colic, vomiting, diarrhea, sexual intercourse, labor, exercise, wakefulness, fasting, and anything that weakens the strength of the body or the organism.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors before that, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines: if it is possible for him to do that.
Then let him enter the bath, if nothing prohibits it: or let him engage in some exercise, so that the blood may be refined.
And place his bloodletting on the chest, around the third hour of the day, as we have said: and strive so that his chest may be evacuated of all harmful animal conditions that day, such as constriction, anger, and fear: and from all bodily conditions, such as labor, pain, and superfluous discharge, and things similar to these.
And let him have present at his seat things with which human custom has run due to their aromatics, odors, and spiciness and similar qualities according to the extent of possibility.
Then let the bloodletter sit on a pillow that is higher than the pillow for bloodletting: afterwards, let him extend his arm, and the bloodletter shall rub it twice or three times with his hand. Then tie the ligature with a strap, and wrap it twice.
And let the binding be moderate, for when the binding is unequal, whether by excess in tightness it impedes the flow of blood; or by looseness, it also impedes the flow of blood.
Then after the binding, it is necessary that the person to be bled friction his hands together near the vein, until the veins swell and become apparent to the sense.
Then the bloodletter should clean the vein with a little oil, in the old proper way.
Then he should place his left index finger on the vein at the spot where he wants to perform the bloodletting, slightly: so that the vein does not retreat, and the strike is not missed. For there are veins that you find like a fleeing string during bloodletting: and among them is one that is full of air: and when you place the lancet on it, it contracts under the lancet, and the bloodletter is deceived, and the lancet does not open the vein: and if it opens it, it is constricted: therefore, the bloodletter must be steady and careful in all these matters.
Then he should put down the lancet. If therefore the vein is opened with one attempt, then it is good. And if not, then repeat it with another attempt slightly below that place or above it quickly: provided the place does not become inflamed.
If it becomes abscessed or the patient swells, then leave him for a day or two days: and do not tighten the bandage. For it may attract the warm abscess. Let him not enter the bath.
Then let him repeat phlebotomy, if he wishes.
If the opening is small, or blood flows thinly: and you fear that the quantity of blood will not come out as you wish: then repeat phlebotomy at the same puncture straightly, and add a little to the procedure.
And do it with haste before the place becomes abscessed, for an abscessed place is in many people a minor operation.
If you see that it is already abscessed, then do not repeat phlebotomy on it at all, for nothing excuses it to you. And place something from the dregs of oil on it, for it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other ointments.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your phlebotomies where you bleed veins to stop the flow of blood: and this is done with mithridate, alfaraque, and sagzenea, when one of them is placed on the spot.
For blood becomes thinner and dissolves when it is thick.
If a large abscess occurs at the site of phlebotomy, for it often happens, especially to one who is not bled except that once, or if the vein opening is small, then hurry and place a sponge soaked in water and salt, slightly heated, on the spot: and bind it for an hour: for it dissolves.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has exited from that vein itself, with its completion, or from another vein.
If some blackness or greenness remains in the place after several days, it does not harm: and if you wish, put something of aloes, or dissolved myrrh on it: or something from the juice of menthastrum, that is, calamus or similar to it.
And many times swellings and eminences occur around the phlebotomy at Basilicae.
Place therefore your hand on it, then if you find softness at the place where you compress it, then that eminence is bad. Be careful, therefore, to put something on it from what we have said. For perhaps the blood of an artery may flow from it.
But make a poultice for it with something that has astringency, so that the area may harden. Then care for it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary to withdraw blood from every person according to the measure of their strength. And what appears from the color dominating over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it flow until it turns red: and similarly if you see it thick: then let it flow until it becomes thin: and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for the one who is full, strong, and requires bloodletting once, to enlarge the vein incision, and the side should be bled: and in the one who is weak, it should be contrary to that.
And it is necessary to draw it from the veins, and that the opening be narrow: and better in bloodletting of a vein is a transverse cut, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is safe from blood flow and nerve cutting: and it is more praiseworthy and safer than bloodletting lengthwise and widthwise.
And for one who is accustomed to faint at bloodletting, it is necessary that they eat before bloodletting a little bread soaked in pomegranate juice: or secaniabin, if it is heated: and you should extract from them blood in three places, or four.
And if it is of a chilled complexion, then let them take before bloodletting bread soaked in honey syrup, or in aromatic honey syrup with spices, or in fragrant aromatic syrup.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting, and the cause is excessive loss of blood: then it is necessary that they drink water with meat, and a delicate fragrant syrup: and use aromatics with algalia, and anoint their chest with it: and use the other things we mentioned in the chapter on fainting due to evacuation.
Who wishes to bleed his arm and release his blood a second time: it is necessary for the one whose bloodletting is for substantial evacuation, and whose strength is weak, to release blood gradually according to the extent of his strength over consecutive days.
Whoever wants to bleed his arm again, and he is strong: then let him do it after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting.
Whoever wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite part to which it declines: it must be bled on the second day or the third.
In whose body there is already much heated blood, and it is sharpened, and causes fever to occur: it is necessary that blood be let from him once, and a great quantity be drawn from him: and the incision should be enlarged until syncope occurs: after you have been preserving all the conditions of bloodletting: and to place your hand on his pulse at the course of the blood, so that death does not occur instead of syncope. For it often happens that the bloodletter is ignorant, and negligence occurs.
And it is not advisable when you want to bleed an arm a second time, and the vein's opening is already closed, and the blood's exit has become difficult, to press on it with violence, and to quickly extend it while bent. For that is very bad; rather, leave it until it is bled a second time.
Or remove with the sharpness of a lancet what has coagulated from the blood at the vein's opening, and place something dissolved salt in water on it. And place something from theriac alfaroch, or sagzenea on it, and apply it with gentle pressure until the blood flows out.
If however already a vein has become an abscess, then leave it alone, and do not cover it, until the abscess settles.
If the necessity provokes to release blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either perform phlebotomy at that same location, or in the other arm in another vein.
The arm is phlebotomized at the location of the Fusa and Basilica veins, when they are not found and are hidden, because it is composed of them.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the patient should insert his hand into warm water until the vein becomes red and appears clearly.
Then it should be tied above it with a bandage with slight constriction in the middle. Then phlebotomize it gradually transversely, not widthwise, nor lengthwise: and let the incision be wide, and let your phlebotomy where you phlebotomize be at the joint of the hand for a moment.
If the flow of blood is prevented, then again put the hand in warm water, and let the blood run in the water until you achieve your purpose.
If, however, you are in the summer days, then you are excused from putting the hand in warm water repeatedly. And indeed, the flow of blood in warm water is greatly utilized during the winter season.
And the phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all other veins because there is no pulsating vein or nerve beneath it.
The phlebotomy of the right hand of Salveta is beneficial for illnesses of the liver.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is to bind the wrist with a bandage, or with your hand, after you introduce it into warm water, until the vein swells and is clearly demonstrated to the sense. Then puncture it transversely. And if you cut it entirely, it will not harm it in any way.
And be careful not to leave your hand with the phlebotomy needle. Beneath it, the nerves of the fingers are exposed without flesh.
Then return the hand to warm water, and let the blood flow into it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood will coagulate at the vein's opening and be prevented from flowing.
Therefore, when you have extracted the necessary amount of blood, then place oil and salt on the vein so that it does not congeal quickly.
And similarly, you should do the same with every tight branch.
The benefit of the phlebotomy of the left hand is that it is helpful for illnesses of the spleen: and similarly perform the phlebotomy of it as you did in the second equally.
The benefit of the phlebotomy of the saphenous vein is for illnesses that are in the lower part of the body, such as ailments of the uterus, retention of menses, diseases of the kidneys, and old sores of the thighs and legs, and similar illnesses.
And the quality of bloodletting is such that the sick person should insert his foot into warm water: and make friction upon it until the veins swell: and bind above the joint of the foot with a ligature. And the place of the veins is near the heel, appearing towards the big toe: and many branches branch out from it on the surface of the foot.
Therefore, bloodletting should be done in the larger branch of it, or near the heel at its convergence: for it is better and safer.
If you bloodlet from it on the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And place your bloodletting instrument across the vein, as if you want to cut it: and let the bloodletter be Nesil.
If however the flow of blood is prevented, then soak his foot in warm water: and allow the blood to flow into it until it is evacuated.
If the bloodletter misses the vein on the first attempt, then repeat the bloodletting slightly higher up, for the area is safe, and you need not fear any harm from it when you are careful of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the Saphena of the other foot equally.
The location of the Sciatic veins, as we have said, is near the heel on the outer side.
And the benefit of its bloodletting is for the pain of gout, when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of the bloodletting is such that the patient should enter the bath and hurry: and bind his leg on the side of the ankle up to four fingers above the heel with a thin long thread: for it does not appear except for this reason.
When therefore it appears, then bloodlet from it according to whatever method is possible for you, either diagonally: and this is better, or cut it with an incision, or sever it with a cut. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most people.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in most people. If you do not find it and it does not appear at all to the senses: then bloodlet from one of its branches, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot, towards the little toe: and be careful of the nerves, and let as much blood flow as you wish.
Then loosen the binding, and place skin over the site of bloodletting, and bind the area. For it heals quickly.
Regarding ventosation, and the quality of its use.
Ventouses are made from horns, and from metal, and from glass.
And ventosation is performed in two ways. With scarification and extraction of blood: the other is ventosation without scarification.
And this ventosation which is without scarification is performed in two ways. For it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And for windy conditions which are treated with scarification and bloodletting, there are fourteen places on the body. One of them is Ventosatio Alnocrati, and it is at the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck: and windy Alhagdahim, and they are two plates on both sides of the neck simultaneously: and windy under the chin, and it is below the jaw on the lower part of the mouth: and windy of the two spatulas: and windy Alachosos at the end of the tail: and windy of the two armpits, and they are in the middle of the two arms: and windy of the two thighs: and windy of the two kneecaps.
And Ventosatio indeed does not draw blood from you from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of this, strength does not diminish from them, as is the case with bloodletting. Nor should ventosatio be administered, whether with fire or without fire, in any diseases that arise from excess, until the whole body is purged.
If necessity provokes to Ventosation due to illness or habit, we administer it at any time at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
This is because there are people for whom when blood multiplies in them until they need its extraction through ventosation, they find heaviness and sodahan in their head.
And among them is one who finds swelling and redness on his face, his head, and his neck. And among them is one who finds itching on his face, and forehead, and darkness, and dullness in his eyes.
And among them is one who scrapes the place of his wind rash. And among them is one whose laughter multiplies. And among them is one who finds the taste of blood in his mouth: or the tongue swells, and he spits out blood. And among them is one whose sleep multiplies. And among them is one who sees in his sleep blood and redness, and the slain, and wounds, and things similar to these.
When therefore we see any of these things, and especially if it is in the middle third of the month: we then prescribe that a wind rash be induced, after two or three hours have passed.
The remedy for wind rash on the neck is: for it helps with heaviness in the head, and what flows to the eyes. But it should be done after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this wind rash is in place of cephalic bloodletting.
And it is not permissible for one of a cold temperament to use it, or for one who has catarrh. For it causes him great harm.
And for that reason, the elderly should not use it, and those who have cold diseases in their head should not use it either.
But he who becomes habitual to it, forgetfulness is generated upon him.
And for that reason, you should instruct the cupper to momentarily place his hand with the cupping vessel towards the lower parts, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Cupping or Alkehel is in place of black bloodletting and general bloodletting, and for that reason it benefits shortness of breath, constriction of breathing, windpipe splitting, cough, and fullness.
And it is necessary to move the cupping vessel Alkehel momentarily because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping with alhagdahim, however, benefits accidental pains in the head, eye diseases, migraines, piercings, and pain in the roots of teeth: and it is in place of general bloodletting.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press his hand with scarification so as not to hit an artery and cause a flow of blood.
Cupping under the chin benefits alcohol in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar diseases that are in the mouth: and it takes the place of bloodletting from the points between the two lips.
Cupping of the spatulae benefits alchafacham, which occurs from obstruction and heat.
Indeed blowing on the inner parts of two flasks provides the same benefit as bloodletting from three veins, Basilica, Fusca, and Cephalica: for it draws blood from all those tiny veins which are in the flesh: and these tiny veins draw from other veins thicker than themselves, until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And you must instruct the Ventosator not to proceed too much in scarification: for the place is devoid of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
The ventosa which is applied over alchausos provides relief to hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And you must instruct the Ventosator to use a large ventosa, and let it be of air: for the place requires strong suction, and perhaps delicate Ventosae may break: and it should be scarified with a large scarification.
Ventosae of the legs provide clear reduction: for they draw blood from the entire body, and alleviate old pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder: they provoke menstruation, and help bothor and ademenul: and serve in place of bloodletting from two saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, they greatly weaken the body and cause syncope to occur in many people.
And the benefit of the Ventosae of alhorcobeni is similar to the benefit of the Ventosae of the two legs.
The quality of the placement of Ventosae is to first place the ventosa empty and draw with moderate suction: and do not prolong the placement of the Ventosae, but place them quickly and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place with equal arrival: and do not cease to repeat and continue this until you see the place already redden and swell, and the redness of blood appear: then scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider in the arrangement of the bodies.
For he who is of tender flesh and sparse pores, you should scarify him once, not more: so that the place does not become ulcerated.
And instruct the bloodletter to enlarge the scarification and make it slightly deep: and to moderate the suction in ease, speed, and gentle motion.
If there is thickness in the blood, then you should scarify him twice. In the first phase, the way is opened for the refinement of blood and its aqueous quality: and in the second phase, for the thorough extraction of thick blood.
If the blood is very feculent, then let there be a third scarification: so as to achieve the ultimate.
And finally, when we want to extract blood gradually or for a short while, one scarification is sufficient for us. If we want to extract much blood, we scarify with multiple scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick: then it should be scarified with deep incisions: and the limit in scarification is the depth of the skin.
What should be administered from oils during the placement of bloodletting, and what the person undergoing bloodletting should beware of.
For him whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, the areas for bloodletting should be anointed with oils that are opening, soothing, and resolving: if it is in the summer season, such as almond oil, or violet oil, or sweet almond oil, or oil from pumpkin seeds.
And if it is in winter time, then use oil of narcissus, or oil of lily, or oil of chamomile, or saffron: and things similar to these.
And if the excess is thick and cold: then let the oil be of rue, or oil of balsamite, or oil of alkanet, or oil of dill: and things similar to these.
And if the windiness is of large pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary to prohibit oil, and these areas should be washed after windiness with rose water, or with cold water, or with water of solatri, or with water of cucumber, or with water of portulaca: and things similar to these.
He whose blood is of much moisture, should wash the windiness with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac: and things similar to these.
Whose excesses are thick, should wash his windiness with old wine, or with water of samsuci, or decoction of dill, or chamomile: and things similar to these.
And it is necessary to be cautious about windiness in a bath, or near the bath; rather, it should be administered an hour or two hours after exiting the bath.
And it is not advisable for someone to sleep after windiness.
What is it that should regulate windiness or bloodletting before windiness.
And after that, you should first consider: then if the windy or bloodlet person is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation overcome his blood: then they should take cooling things, such as pomegranate syrup: and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and sikanjabin, and rue: and things similar to these.
And place his foods chickens, and sheep meats, and prepared foods with herbs: and things similar to these.
And for one whose complexion is cold, he should take in drink syrup of honey, or of almaua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds: and he should take fragrant wine that is between old and new: and he should be instructed to use moderation in food. And place his food chickens, and canebir: and sparrows, that is, small birds: and pigeons should be seasoned with issiad.
And it should be that the drink on the day of bloodletting and venesection is more than food: and it should be that in some men's drinks there is given during certain hours tyriaca alfaroch, or medicine of musk, that is, muscata, or alesselithe before bloodletting: and before phlebotomy, or after: so that the principal members are strengthened, and the blood is refined. And they should not take it in heated drink.
Regarding Windiness without Scarification. Windiness without scarification, however, is Windiness that is placed upon the liver, and spleen, and gout, and upon the stomach, navel, and areas of the kidneys, and the anus: because these members do not tolerate scarification upon them.
And they are not sought for anything else except the transmission of blood from one member to another: such as placing Windiness upon the breasts in the illness of nasal bleeding. And we use them to resolve cold invisible swelling in the limb: just as we place swollen on the belly, and navel. For it rarefies the limb, and heats it, and removes pain because it resolves that swelling.
