It is also placed on the kidneys when obstruction or stone occurs in them. For by the power of its attraction, it may open obstruction or remove the stone from its place.
And it similarly acts when placed on the liver and spleen for swelling to be treated in both of them.
And these swellings are treated empty with only incision. And sometimes they are treated with fire.
And sometimes they are used full of warm water in pleurisy ailments: and this is done by filling the cup with hot water alone, or water in which herbs suitable for the condition have been boiled.
The method of applying the cupping vessel with fire.
Let the diameter of its opening be two fingers wide, as we have formed it: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be a small hole on its side at about the middle, according to the size through which a needle can enter: it should be made of copper or citrine, with thick, smooth, equal, clean edges: so that it does not harm the limb when placed upon it: and let there be a transverse rod of copper or iron in its center where the candle with fire is placed.
And now it is made very large and inflated, larger than we mentioned, and slightly smaller than that, according to the illnesses and age of the one who uses it.
For the bellows of children and those who are thin are different from the bellows of men and those with thick bodies.
The quality of placing this bellows with fire on the limb is to light a strip of linen with fire properly, or a small candle from wax: and place it in the middle of the crossbar which is in the middle of the bellows: so that the fire is lit around the lower part of the bellows, so that the body of the sick person is not burned.
Then it is placed on the limb: and a finger should be over the hole that I mentioned, until when we hold the bellows: while we need to, remove the finger, and vapor will escape through that hole, and the bellows will immediately release.
Then light the linen strip according to its manner, and repeat it, if you need to do so.
In the bellows which is used for the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no crossbar, nor a hole: and it is not used except that it is filled with water and placed on the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this bellows, the larger it is, so that it can hold much water, is better.
Concerning the
Leeches are not administered to many parts, except those members where the placement of cups is not possible due to their small size, such as the lip, and gum, and similar areas. Or on parts denuded of flesh, such as the finger, and nose, and similar areas.
And the quality of using them is: to use leeches that are from sweet waters, clean from putrefactions.
Then let them be released day and night in sweet water until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their stomachs. Then the body should be purged first with bloodletting and with cupping. Then cleanse the patient's limb until it reddens. Then place them on it.
Therefore, when they are filled and fall off, and it is possible to apply suction with a cupping vessel at the same location, then this is ultimately more beneficial: and if not, then cleanse the area with vinegar. Then with plenty of water, it should be rubbed and squeezed.
If the flow of blood continues after the leeches fall off and it seeps out, then soak a linen cloth in cold water and place it on top until the seeping stops.
If the blood flows excessively, then sprinkle powdered cumin or gallnuts, or similar astringents on it until the bleeding stops.
Or let halves of peeled beans be placed on the site: and leave them until the beans adhere in place, for the blood is congealed.
And it is necessary if the repetition of bloodletting is required, when possible, to change the places.
If bloodletting is prohibited due to suspense, then the area should be cleaned with fresh blood. Or pierce with a needle in the places until some blood comes out. Then they should be applied, for they adhere immediately when they sense some blood.
When you want them to fall off, sprinkle something on them from ground aloe, or salt, or ash: and they will fall off immediately.
This Chapter is also often necessary in the art of Medicine, and it is the restoration of fractured and dislocated bones.
Know, O Sons, that foolish doctors and the common folk boast about this Chapter, and those who have never inspected an ancient book or read a letter from it.
And for this reason, this method is deprived from the sciences in our land. And I did not comply with that good at all, and I have not attained from it what I have attained unless due to the length of reading ancient books, and studying their understanding, until I extracted knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experience and study throughout my life.
And now I have described to you from that in this Chapter all that my knowledge contains: and beyond what passed my experience, after I shortened it for you, and freed it from the burden of lengthiness, and abbreviated it to the point of abbreviation, and showed it to the extent of deviation: and I formed in it many forms of instruments used in it, as it is with the addition of deviation, just as I did in the two preceding Chapters.
Chapter II.
We must say in the prologue of this Chapter the summaries of the discourse and the chapters, to whose understanding the artisan is first compelled to stand upon their truth: namely, so that you and those who are quick may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or torsion, or fall happens to someone, then you must first hasten to bleed him and loosen him, or both at the same time: unless something prohibits it, such as weakness of strength; or he to whom one of these things happened is a child or an infirm old man; or it is a time of extreme heat or severe cold.
Then he should be content with his food, cold vegetables, and meats of birds, and goats: and forbid wine, and fatty meats, and to fill up with foods and all food which fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from a warm abscess, and you do not fear the discharge of matter to the limb: then the sick person should return to his initial regimen with which he was accustomed.
When therefore the broken bone begins to heal, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed nutrient foods with much, thick, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity: such as food made from cooked wheat with water, and roots, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar things. With this regimen, the fracture is healed more quickly and better.
And you should know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in their early old age, and the infirm, cannot be joined and consolidated according to their nature always, because of the dryness of their limbs: and they consolidate and join those which are at the end of softening, like the bones of small children.
However, nature connects over the broken bone from all sides something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds it, until they are glued together, and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prohibits it from its functions: and for this reason it is necessary that you provide the sick person's nourishment with foods in which there is heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And you shall know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of limbs: for the fracture of the leg is different from the fracture of the skull bone: and the fracture of the chest bone is different from the fracture of the back bone: and similarly all other limbs exist distinct from each other: and I come with a recollection of every type of fracture exposed in its Chapter distinguished from another. And the types of fractures of the bone also differ among themselves.
For its fracture is arched, besides which shards may occur in it. And sometimes its fracture is along the length of the bone: and its fractures are splintered, that is, shards, and projections proceeding, as they are in a reed, when it is cut with a knife for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a fracture, a small fissure.
And every type has its own method or instrument for its restoration, according to which its recollection will come, distinguished in their respective places.
And by which the fracture of the bone is recognized is its deformity and its prominence: and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no apparent deformity in the place, nor sound at your touch when you touch the bone with movement, nor does the patient find great pain: then there is no fracture there, but it is possible that there is a deformity or a small fissure: therefore it is not necessary to move it with hand and compression at all, but place upon it medicines of which its recollection comes, after the moment that befits the place: then bind with a fine bandage.
And you should know that when the site is curved and split into two, besides the infections that may occur in it, nevertheless all parts are removed from their companion: then it is necessary for you to begin at your appointed time for its correction, evening out itself before a warm abscess occurs.
And if a warm abscess occurs in it, then leave it for several days until the abscess rests. Then level it according to whichever method can be done based on its tenderness and nature.
And you should know that the restoration and leveling of it is easier than that of bone, in which infections occur.
Then treat it according to how the memory of its treatment comes to you.
And if there are infections in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this fractured limb from both parts, if it is a hand or foot: or with your hand if the limb is small: or with two ropes, or with one rope and your hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb on an equal site according to its natural shape until the parts of the fractured limb are extended: then strive to return those additions with all ingenuity and in whatever way you can to their places: and beware according to your strength, lest excessive pain or injury occur through your operation: and strive according to your strength to apply one of the two bones with its companion in the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time for you to attempt and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and equalize it according to the extent of your skill.
And beware strong extension and forceful compression, as many foolish ones do. For they often cause a hot abscess or contraction in the limb through their operation: as we have already seen many times from their procedures.
Then apply after equalization, and proper operation, and constriction of that limb, and rest, and tranquility: and advise the patient about it. And make him beware not to move it during his waking hours, and in his sleep regarding his turning, and stirring, and in his excretion, and all his movements with his utmost strength. And let him beware that the position of the limb is not one that causes pain. Which means that when he feels pain or injury in the positioning of the limb: then it is necessary to change it to another position where he does not feel pain.
And beware that its position is equal and straight so that no distortion occurs in the limb when it is restored.
And the nature of the constriction of the fractured limb is as I narrate.
Know that fractured limbs differ in their smallness, and their magnitude, and their form.
Therefore, those among them that are small, such as the arm, and the finger, and the toe, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of a soft, fine cloth. Which of them are thick, like the thigh and leg, must have a wide, hard wrapping.
For the wide bandage connects the large limb and tightens it evenly from all sides so that no spot enters it.
Then begin after your evacuation to apply an appropriate liniment to that place, on soft tow over the site of the fracture. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the site of the fracture with three or four layers, according to what the limb deserves: and hold your hand firmly with the bandage.
Then proceed with it to the upper part from the site of the fracture, and hold less tightly than your previous tightness with which you tightened the fractured site. Then move away with the wrapping from the site of the fracture gradually, and loosen the tightness little by little until you reach a large part from the healthy area.
Then take another strip and wrap it again over the fractured area with layers. Then proceed with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture. And let your work be in the tightness of the wrapping and its loosening, according to what we said in the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings with soft tow or cloth, with which the bent part of the fracture is aligned, if there is a bend: and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another strip over it. Then place the properly made splints over these wraps immediately, if there is no swelling or hot abscess in the area.
If there is swelling or an abscess, then place on it something that will sit on the abscess and reduce the swelling; and leave it for several days. Then tighten the splints over it.
And the splints should be made from the halves of a wide, shaved, and wisely formed cane, or they should be made from the wood used for sieves, which is made from pine, or geraid, that is, palm branches, or calingi: and the width of each splint, according to this form, should have the same shape.
However, it is necessary that the splint which is placed over the traction be thicker and wider than the other splints: and the length of the splint should be according to what is suitable for the member in size and smallness.
Then bind over the splints with another strip according to the same tightness as the first. Then bind it with threads properly, according to what we have said about tightness: and it is that your binding over the site of the fracture should be tighter: and the further away from the fracture, the looser the binding should be.
And it is necessary that the threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and they should be made of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the work of foolish ones who use twisted linen cords, it is a great mistake: for the binding becomes too tight with them beyond what is suitable.
And thin threads again do not suit: because you do not achieve with them what you want through tension.
And it should not be less than a finger's width between the beam and the beam.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the beams after they are tightened at healthy sites: then place under them soft tow or carded wool until nothing is harmed from that.
When there is a fracture with a wound, and a rupture occurs in the skin, I will come to it with its description in the second chapter due to its particularity.
And you should know that not every fractured limb should be bound with beams on the first day. This is because when the limb is large, you should not place beams on it until after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the risk of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters with which the ancients restored fractures and dislocations.
 Method of a shortened common plaster that restores fractures, suitable for most complexions, especially women and children: because it is such that neither heat nor cold overcomes it.
And it is done by taking the millstone dust, which is the flour residue that hangs on the walls of the mill near the motion of the millstone: and prepare it as it is, except that you sieve it with egg white: and set its consistency not too thick nor too thin. Then use it.
Method of another plaster for restoring fracture, dislocation, and torsion. Take almess, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugwort: and the juice of all  X, myrrh, and aloes both  V, and alethil  XX, pound them all, sift them, and mix them with alethil water: or with egg white, if the constitution of the patient is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
Method of another plaster which also benefits fractures and torsions. Take mugwort, and mess, and white hollyhock, of all  X, myrrh, and aloes both  V, acacia  VI, Armenian bole  XX, pound them all with thorough grinding, sift them, and prepare them with water or egg white: and administer them.
Method of a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places: and alleviates pain occurring in them, and bone cuts, and fractures. Take suet wool, and soak it in vinegar and boiled oil: and place it on the area.
And in this plaster there is no power of restoration, but it is good for soothing a hot abscess: and it properly relieves pain.
The method of the plaster which restores the broken bone. Take the leaves of the fig of the deaf, and the leaves of the wild poppy: and grind all, and make a plaster from both.
The method of the shortened plaster, applied for the restoration of a fractured bone: and when you want the dissolution of the remaining abscess. Take the root of althea, and chamomile, and flowers of violet, and flour of the herb, of all one part each: grind all, and prepare with wine, if the limb is not heated.
If it is heated, then prepare it with water of humid coriander, or with water, and use it.
The method of another stronger plaster for dissolution, than this first one, when a hard abscess occurs at the restoration of the limb. Take the root of althea, and seeds of flax, and pomegranate flowers, and sweet clover, and mallow, and flowers of violet, and chamomile, of all one part each: grind all, and prepare with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. Use all of this according to the heat of the limb, and the cooling thereof.
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration, except for a plaster made of wax and oil: nothing else: and he described it to be a balance between thickness and fineness.
But Galen thought that over the fractured limb during its restoration there should be placed things which have a drying heat, such as myrrh, and aloes, and frankincense, and similar to them of those we have mentioned.
Method of the plaster that helps with the injury and pain. Take myrrh, and chickpeas, and human hair cut into pieces, or bird feathers, and coltsfoot, and salt in equal parts: they are to be ground, sifted, and prepared: and a plaster is to be made from it.
The amount according to which the bandage should remain, and then be removed, is such that you should consider whether there is no pain, nor itching, nor movement of the fractured bone from its place: then do not remove it for many days. If there occurs in the place a violent itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling: then hurry and remove it immediately, and take off the plaster from him: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, wet: and submerge it in warm water, and wash the area with it until the itching subsides, and the pain calms.
If there occurs in the place a violent itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling: then hurry and remove it immediately, and take off the plaster from him: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, wet: and submerge it in warm water, and wash the area with it until the itching subsides, and the pain calms. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then place wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, on it: and bind it there for one night, until you are sure there is no hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then apply it to a fine binding, and a small plaster: and do not bind it tightly with your first bandage, and keep it fine until it heals.
If you see that the abscess, and redness, and pain, and swelling, and all symptoms are now completely removed, and you need a plaster and binding, then treat it again as you did at first equally.
If nothing of what we have mentioned has occurred to the limb, then do not wash it unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is left for twenty days, do everything according to how the limb appears to you from its washing, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and the coagulation of flesh approximates over it: then also add more to the binding than your whole first binding: and also increase the food of the patient according to his previous recollection.
If you see the place of the fracture already drying out and becoming thinner than it should be: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. So wash it with warm water every time you clean it, and do this for all three days, and lighten the binding slightly.
Through this operation, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what foolish restorers do with a fractured limb by another method when it is not restored at first as it should be, and is restored crookedly, is an error from their operation, and a great fear: and if it were right, the ancients would have said so in their books and acted upon it.
And I have not seen any trace of them doing that at all, and it is better that it not be done.
On the treatment of an accident to the head.
The types of fractures are diverse, and its forms are many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture caused by a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone entirely until it reaches the saphac, which is under the bone, just as an axe affects wood: and because of this, this type of fracture is called Axial.
Or the sword cuts through part of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the cut does not penetrate to the end of it: and this type of fracture is called Kalan, that is, Absolute Elevation.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is a grinding or wearing down: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or falling on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also penetrating, which approaches the membrane that is under the bone: or it is on the surface of the bone: and it is a wound of these two fractures, either small or large.
And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone like a hair's subtlety: and it is a small crack, and because of this, this type of fracture is called Capillary.
And among them is a fracture that occurs from a fall or a blow with a stone, or similar things, which cause the bone plate to intrude inward, and a concavity is formed: like what happens to copper basins when they are struck and part of them indents inward.
And that happens most especially in the heads of moist bones, as are the heads of children.
And separate attempts are made for all these kinds of fractures, and I will come with a recollection of the whole of that in its proper place.
And we know all these kinds of fractures by examining them, and investigating them with probes, and removing the corrupted flesh that is on them.
The capillary kind is recognized by this, that it is discovered on the bone, and cleaned, and anointed over it with encaustic: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, first consider the symptoms of the patient: then if you see from the symptoms that it clearly indicates fear, such as vomiting of cholera, and stiffness, and loss of consciousness, and cessation of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar ones among the symptoms in most conditions, doubtless it is not cured: and if you see symptoms that do not frighten you, and hope for recovery: then begin his treatment.
Which means, if a wounded person comes to you right after being wounded: and it is during the winter months, then it is necessary that you focus on the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all conditions.
And if it is during the summer months, then it is necessary that you hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone from the membrane becomes corrupted, and those symptoms we have mentioned occur.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and it is with fragments, and with traction: then it is necessary that you cut away the shattered and worn part, according to what I tell you.
And it is as though you shave the head of the wounded: and expose over the bone in whatever way possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light over the injury.
If it happens that during the exposure of the bone there is a flow of blood or a warm abscess: then apply what is necessary to it. And it is as though you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil: and make the wound cease, until the abscess settles, and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin in the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with an instrument of sharp edge: and this is its form.
Then apply another cutter after the narrower one for a moment: and this is its form.
Then apply yet another cutter wider than the second one.
And the second method is to have a number of different cutters with you: some of which are wider than others, and some of which are shorter than others: and let them be sharp at their ends, and let them be made of iron or steel: and use them easily in striking over the bone, so that it does not shake the head and harm it.
If the bone is strong, hard: then you must pierce around it before you administer the cutting with drills which are called non-penetrating drills: and they are not so named unless because they do not pass through the boundary of the fleshy bone to that which is behind it: therefore, the drill has a round end above that which is under its sharp head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take from these drills a great number, each of which suits the thickness of the bone, until you have a drill suitable for every skull: whose sharp end's length and brevity should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three species of drills, or trephines.
The quality of piercing around the fractured bone is: to place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been penetrated. Then change the drill to another place, and place it so that there is the thickness of a rod or about that between every hole.
Then cut with the knives what is between every hole in the bone: and make it finer than you can on that from the end of lightness, as we said, until you root out the bone, either by hand or with some other of the instruments you have prepared for this: such as tweezers, and delicate chisels.
And you must be cautious with all care that the drill or the knife does not touch anything from the sinew.
When therefore you circle around the hole by piercing and it is separated from the siphac, if it is continuous with it, and you extract it, you must scrape and level the end of what remains in the bone with another similar cutting instrument. However, it must be thinner and finer than other cutting instruments.
If any part of the small bones remains and is troublesome, then remove it easily with the instrument you have. Then care for the wound after that with licinium and the ointments we have mentioned.
And another method of the operation is easy, entirely free from fear: which Galen mentioned and praised highly: and this is his saying. It is necessary for you first to begin uncovering the part of the bone where the fracture is more severe and evident: until when you have uncovered that part, and placed under it the end of this lens-shaped cutting instrument, which has this form.
And the lens-shaped part, being blunt, does not cut anything: and the sharp edge of it running along the two sides in length, as you see: so that the lens-shaped part is placed against the siphac, with its sharp face cutting into the bone.
Then strike on the cutting instrument from one side with a small mallet until you cut through the entire bone easily, as it turns: and you shall be secure about the flap, that no accident happens to it which you fear entirely.
And if the medic were not more foolish and ignorant than others, nor asleep, then if anything of the flap remained attached to the bone in some places, he would cut it away from the bone with the end of the lens-shaped cutting instrument, and free it easily. For it is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the panniculus, and what is severed from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and subtle thorns remain in it: then you must rasp that roughness and root out those thorns with delicate rasps of which you have prepared a number of various sizes, so that you may be able to administer the most suitable and correct ones of them according to what the operation leads you to, and the bone itself, and its shape. And you must administer according to your judgment, with which of the rasps you first rasp the bone, wider with those rasps. Then use the finer one after it.
And you must administer according to your judgment, with which of the rasps you first rasp the bone, wider with those rasps. Then use the finer one after it. And do not cease to do that continuously until you reach the point of administering the finest and most subtle of them all.
In each of the other small capillary fissures and subtle fractures, you must administer care according to what leads to its correction.
And it is a matter that is not hidden from anyone who has any interest in this art, and stands on what we have written and shown in great fractures.
If the panniculus detected is retained when you rasp the bone: then you must take a piece of linen according to the size of the wound, and soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another piece of cloth, doubled or tripled, and soak it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and make it as light as you can on top of it so that it is not too heavy.
Then apply over the broad ligament; and do not tighten it except to the extent that it holds the wound only: and leave it with this care for one day or two days, until you are secure from a warm abscess. Then remove it, and apply after it medicines with which there is excessive dryness: such as lily roots, and flour of the herb, and frankincense powder, and aristolochia, and similar to them: and it is that you make from these medicines a powder, which is sprinkled over the wound when they are dry.
And universally apply to it all medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to irritate. And beware according to your ability, that the wound in your care be clear: neither let there be in it ointment dirtiness, nor filth.
And beware according to your ability, that the wound in your care be clear: neither let there be in it ointment dirtiness, nor filth. And do not allow pus to accumulate in it at all: for when pus accumulates on the brain covering, it corrupts and putrefies it, and great harm befalls the patient thereafter: and it often happens in baldness of the head, when the bone is cleansed from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackening appears on its surface.
Therefore, consider then, if the patient experiences the conditions we have mentioned, you shall know that it is certainly perilous.
And if blackening does not occur except from the medicine that is applied to it, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackening: then you must take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and line the cloth with them. Then place him on a shiffin: afterwards care for him with suitable treatments until he is healed.
On the restoration of the nose when fractured.
You should know that only the two upper parts of the nose, or one of them, can be fractured; because there are two bones in these areas. Since the lower part is made of cartilage, it does not fracture but may suffer abrasion, distortion, and flattening.
If one of the two parts is fractured, then you should insert a small finger into the nostril and align the fracture with your index finger and thumb from the outside until the nose returns to its natural form: this should be done skillfully and easily. And beware that your operation does not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then it should be aligned using the tip of a slender probe.
If the fracture is in both parts, then do the same: and hurry with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day when the inflammation has subsided.
Then insert a strip of linen cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part: or insert two strips if the fracture is in both parts: and let the strip be thick enough to fill the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said that licinia should be infused in butter, and changed every day. That indeed does not seem right to me; rather, it is necessary that licinium be infused in the white of an egg, mixed with millstone powder. Then apply the licinium until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of licinium, after it is wrapped with a soft cloth: and it happens that it holds the nasal fracture more strongly, and does not prevent the patient from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary. If you wish, do that: and if you wish, use licinia.
If it happens that the nose is affected again while you are treating a warm abscess, apply plasters to the nose with cerote, or with suet infused in rose oil, or a little of dyaquilon.
If, however, a warm abscess does not occur, then it is necessary to apply plasters made with sesame flour and incense powder mixed with the white of an egg. Then place a soft cloth over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are fractured slightly or crushed, then it is necessary to cut over them and extract them with instruments that are suitable for them. Then sew the incision, and treat it with what promotes fleshing and strengthening, using appropriate plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nose, then it is necessary to treat it with lint: and administer a lead tube until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not with a wound: then look if the fracture is on the outer side only, and it is not broken into two, and it curves inward: then its diagnosis is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the patient: similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand: and push with it the prominence of the fracture from inside with ease to the outside with your other hand from the inner part of the bone properly, and with it equalize it.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to apply traction from both sides, according to straightness: until its equalization is possible.
If, however, the teeth have already been loosened or broken: then bind those that you hope will remain with gold thread, or silver thread, or silk. Then place wax over the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and put a properly made large splint over the cloth: or a piece of sole leather equal in length to the jaw.
Then bind it from above according to how its bandage is prepared for you and apply it suitably until it is not destroyed: and instruct the sick person to be calm and at rest: and give his food mild drinks.
If however you think that something has already changed in its appearance to some extent: then hurry and remove it on the third day. Then correct what has been altered from it, and make a poultice of mill dust with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the wax: and place soft wool over the poultice.
While therefore the poultice remains attached to it and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it alone, do not remove it until it heals: or bind the fracture. For this fracture is often bound for three weeks.
If during this time a warm abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times for its reduction, until the abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, take note. For if a fragment is already separated from the bone or from the socket: then be meticulous in this, to remove those fragments from the socket with what is suitable for you among the instruments for their removal.
If however the wound opening is narrow, then enlarge it with a lancet according to the amount necessary for you.
Then when you have removed those from the socket and nothing remains of them, sew the wound opening if it is large: and if not, apply a poultice suitable for it to promote fleshing until it heals.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
The collarbone is most often fractured on the front side near the shoulder, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it is either broken and separated into two parts, besides which contusions may occur: and it is easier to restore it.
Or it is such that contusions occur in the bone: and it is more difficult to restore.
Or it is such that there is a fracture with a wound.
And the procedure when the fracture is without a wound is to have two assistants present, so that one of them holds the support following the broken collarbone, and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers until its form is as it should be: and there should not be any prominence or concavity in it.
If you need more extension, then it is necessary to place a sphere of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit. The size should be according to your requirement. Extend and elevate the collarbone, and compress the sphere with your hand until you equalize the fracture as needed.
If you cannot pull the end of the collarbone outward because it has sunk deeply, then the patient must lie down on their back. Place a moderately sized pillow under their shoulder and have an assistant press the shoulder downward until the sunken part of the collarbone is raised upward. Then correct and equalize the fracture with your fingers.
If however you feel a splinter from the collarbone and it moves: then it is necessary to cut over it and remove the splinter with ease.
If however it is retained in the bone, then skill is needed in cutting it with one of the files which you have prepared for this, after you place under the collarbone the instrument that keeps sifach: and it is an instrument made of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon that does not have a concavity.
And its width should be according to what you need from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length should also be according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And it should have two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide, and the other narrow.
If however the disruption of the wound which you made when you extracted the bone splinter is wide, and it is threatened by a warm abscess: then bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a warm abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and lint according to the amount of the wound's tear.
If a warm abscess should occur, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it on top of it.
A narrowing of the bone when it is without a wound and without a cut is such that you place on the bone a poultice made from mill flour with egg white. And place a soft tow on it. Then place the ball under his scrotum, if you need that. Then take the himematem, that is, the roll which the Saracens use, which should be very long: and its width should be one palm, or about that. Then place a doubled poultice on the tow and plaster. Then take a strip from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in a cloth. Then wrap the cloth with the strip in the place of the himematem that falls over the fracture and area. Then place the himematem over the fracture as it is rolled, and wrap it around his neck, and under his healthy scrotum, and under his injured scrotum: and adjust it over all parts, and in a way that you see the binding holds the fracture tightly: and this is what should not be hidden from you, and everything should be such that the strip does not move off the broken area. And for this reason, it is necessary to look after the patient every day. Whenever the bandage softens and you see that the strip can now be removed: then adjust it, and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's scrotum on his back, and put a small pillow under his scrotum at night, so that his support is lifted from his side with it. The fracture of the fork should be raised by elevating the shoulder.
Either bind his arm to his neck: and do not loosen the bandage, unless an accident occurs from itching or from a swelling, until twelve days have passed.
Then renew the poultice, if you see that it is needed. And add to the binding, and leave it until it is restored and healed.
And the fracture of the fork is bound and strengthened as much as possible within twenty-eight days: and sometimes it happens in some men in a shorter time.
Regarding the restoration of the fracture of the spatula.
The spatula is rarely fractured, and if it is, it occurs at its wide part: and only the edges of it are broken.
When therefore that part is fractured, and it is broken in the middle of it, and it is not recognized except by touch: then according to the shape of the fracture attempt its preparation. And restore it to its natural shape in any way that is possible for you.
Then apply flour dust mixed with egg white to the area, and place a soft cloth over it, and cover with a poultice made of doubled cloth. Then place a wide strip from a thin board over it according to the size of the entire spatula, or slightly larger than it.
If however there is a concavity at some place of the Spatula, then equalize that concavity with soft tow, until the astella descends according to equality.
Then bind over it with linen bandages, with appropriate constriction, and the firmness of the astella, until it is not moved from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage is softened, tighten it, and equalize the astella if it moves from its place: and let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And the Spatula is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When this number is completed, then loosen the bandage: and you will be in safety. For it is made of bones whose union is not feared, nor their separation.
If a fragment protrudes from the bone and pierces beneath the skin: then cut over it, and remove it. And do as we have said in the case of a clavicle fracture from the reduction of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
On the restoration of a fractured rib.
The chest is sometimes fractured in the middle of it, and this rarely occurs. Its extremities are frequently fractured and crushed from its accidents.
When its middle is fractured, which inclines towards the lower parts, and there occurs severe pain to it, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps he vomits blood, and a concavity is declared in the broken bone, and it is not hidden from the sense, Its restoration is that the patient should lie supine on his back: and place between his spatulas a pillow.
Then press his shoulders, and bring the ribs together from both sides or sides with your hands: and adjust in its alignment, in every way that it is possible and appropriate, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then place a poultice and lint on it, and put an oblong piece of thin board from willow or chalking, or those similar in lightness over it after you wrap it in cloth. Then adjust in binding it over the fractured bone, so that it does not move: and go with the bandage around to the back several times, and tie it with a proper knot.
Then examine the bandage at all times, and whenever it loosens, tighten it: and if the necessity of it provokes its removal due to itching, or corrosion which occurs in the area, or pain, or swelling: then hurry and remove it, and take off the poultice, and correct what happens with its method of cure.
Then apply the poultice again if you see it is necessary: and attach it with a knot until it heals.
On the restoration of ribs when they are fractured.
Know that the fracture of the ribs does not occur except in the thick parts following the back, and from their extremities on the front side. And attrition does not occur to them unless because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense during the examination with fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that the fracture is evenly aligned with fingers according to the possible manner, until the shape is even, as it should be. Then apply plasters and bind the fractured ends with a splint, if it requires that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is inclined inward: then the patient suffers severe pain and puncturing, like the puncturing that occurs to one who has pleurisy: therefore, because the bone pierces the membrane, and also the patient suffers difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting much blood: and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have devised many ingenious methods for this: among them are those who say that you should give the patient foods that generate inflammation and flatulence, so that the abdomen becomes inflamed, expands, and pushes the fracture outward.
We, however, abhor making a quick cause to produce a hot abscess unless it occurs. If, however, it has already occurred, then address it accordingly.
And some of them said: place something ventosa over the area, then suck it with strength, and it is similar in reasoning. However, it is feared that it attracts windy excesses to the place due to the condition of weakness.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been submerged in warm oil: and poultices should be placed between the ribs, until it is filled: so that the bandage is even when you wrap it according to the roundness.
Then the patient should be treated for pleurisy with food and medicine.
If a strong thing that the patient cannot tolerate constrains the patient, and the wound pierces the membrane with a damaging puncture, and we are concerned for the patient: then it is necessary to cut above the location and expose from the broken rib. Then place upon it the instrument that preserves the seam, the memory of which has preceded: and cut the bone with ease, and cut into it.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound if it is large with stitches: and treat it with plasters until it heals.
If a hot abscess should occur in the meantime, then hurry and infuse poultices in rose oil, and place them on the area: and treat the patient with what is suitable for the abscess.
In the meantime, he should also lie on the side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Regarding the restoration of fractured vertebrae of the back and neck.
Fractures sometimes occur in the neck vertebrae, and wear rarely happens to them: and similarly with the vertebrae of the back as well. When therefore this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not healed: then have confidence. For if you see his hands already softened, and numbed, and dead: and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them: and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel that, and does not feel pain in them: then you know that he will not be healed, and in most conditions it is mortal.
If he moves them and feels the contraction with fingers and the pricking: then you know that the neck of the bone is safe, and that the patient will be healed with treatment.
If it happens to the vertebrae of the back in a similar manner, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not: then examine his feet: if you see that they are softened, and what happened to the hands also happens to them: then when he lies on his back, flatulence and excretion occur involuntarily: and when he turns onto his stomach, excretion occurs involuntarily: and when he turns onto his back and wants to urinate but cannot: then you know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in his treatment.
If however nothing of that happens to him, the case is lighter.
And the treatment for what happens from that is to attempt the reduction of the abscess with heat: so that you place on the vertebra crushed rose oil alone: or with eggs of a roasted owl.
Place it upon the area several times a day until the hot inflammation subsides; then apply one of the strengthening, drying, and binding plasters to the site and bind it with a bandage; and instruct the patient to use rest and calm. Nor should he sleep on the side where he feels pain until it is healed.
If it happens near the worn bone or something that is already separated from it, then you must cut over the skin and remove it. Then bring together the two edges of the wound with a suture if it is large. Then treat it with some flesh-forming ointment until it heals.
If the last bone of the spinal column is fractured, and it is the end of the tail: then you must insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible, and perform the second alignment.
Then place it on the area and remove it; and treat the wound with the previous treatment until it heals.
Regarding the restoration of the anchor.
Chapter XI.
The bones of the ankles are rarely fractured. If they are fractured, then the fracture is not complete but a comminution at their extremities: and they are split lengthwise and displaced inward: and the patient experiences pain in the area, and there is bruising: and the leg that is fractured becomes numb.
And the restoration is such that you extend your hand over it until you stand over the fracture how its figure is.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then equalize that fracture according to what is prepared for you by equalization until it resembles its natural figure.
If the fracture is along the length or already inclines towards the interior, then make the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared. When therefore you have equalized it, place a plaster on it; then place a stick of wood or leather on it; and bind with a bandage on which you do not fear the displacement of the fracture nor the removal of the stick.
And equalize the concavity of it with what fills it until it is held by the bandage according to equality; and instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
If a warm abscess occurs to him, then cease from extending it and its restoration until the warm abscess subsides; and place on it what makes it subside as precedes, then return to its restoration; and bind it as appropriate.
If there is pus in the bone or if it is crushed, extract it.
Regarding the restoration of the helper's fracture.
The helper is that which is between the elbow and the head of the spatula.
If therefore it is fractured, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: to take a piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form that follows.
And let two bands be tied at the two ends.
Then it should be suspended from an elevated place: and the patient should sit on a seat. Then he should throw his fractured arm over the wood, until it reaches the armpit, where the curvature of the wood is attached in the middle.
Then hang something heavy above it, so that it may stretch downward. Then the physician equalizes the fracture with his hands simultaneously, until the fracture returns to its proper position.
And the other method is to have the patient lie on their back: and suspend his arm from his neck with a band. Then command two assistants, so that one of them holds what is above the fracture with his hands: and the other holds below it, and each extends to their respective part.
And if you wish for a stronger extension, then bind below the fracture with one band: and above it with another band: and each of the two assistants should extend them to their respective parts.
If therefore the fracture is near the end of the shoulder, then it is necessary to place one band under the armpit, and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a bandage be placed on that place, and on the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease without effort, until when the fracture is equalized as it should be, and is applied with good application: then bind it, if a warm abscess does not occur.
If a warm abscess occurs, then release the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess quiets: then bind it: and the method of binding it is to place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster. Then let the arm be bound with support, and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth and bind the arm with support, so that the arm stays in its proper place: if nothing prohibits you from it, and nothing is altered from the fractured bone.
If you fear that something may be altered from the fractured bone, then use splints: and it is done by placing a splint over the fracture itself which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place the width of a finger between each splint: and the length of the splints should be according to the fracture with an additional three fingers on each side.
Then bind over the splints with the bandage as I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your binding on the place of the fracture should be tighter. And the more the fracture is elongated, the lesser the binding should be.
If you see the placement of splints and binding as we said, at the hour when you restore the limb immediately, then do so: and if you fear a hot abscess, then remove the binding and splints until the seventh day, as we said.
Then observe the bandage for all three days, so that itching or swelling does not occur, or food is prevented from reaching the limb due to the excess tightness of the binding. Therefore, correct all of that according to what we said.
If however you are confident that nothing will happen from it: then do not loosen the bandage unless after three days or more.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his hand on his stomach: and place under the support a cushion filled with wool evenly.
And observe at every hour of the night or day that the shape of the fractured limb is not loosened or the bandage softened. Therefore, correct all of that with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we have mentioned, so that the food is light at first, until the wound begins to heal: then it is necessary to make the food coarser.
For it is customary that the support and leg be bound for forty days: then it should be loosened, and a bath administered, and treated with ointments that correct it.
If the fracture is foul and ground down: then do not remove the bandage and splint until fifty days, and until two months.
On the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two forearm bones.
One of them is smaller: and it is the one that follows the thumb. And the other is larger, and it is the one that is placed below the smaller one on the lower side.
Perhaps only the larger forearm bone is fractured, or the smaller one: and perhaps both are fractured at the same time.
When the smaller forearm bone is fractured above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower forearm bone is fractured, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And it is worse when both bones are fractured at the same time.
If the bone that is fractured is the upper smaller forearm bone, then it is appropriate for the doctor, in its restoration, to apply a slight extension with ease, until it is straightened.
If the larger forearm bone is the one that is fractured: then it is necessary to apply a much stronger binding.
And it is necessary to position the hand in shape during its restoration, and to extend it on a pillow. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the lower joint be common to all the fingers or the rest: and the patient sitting squarely on himself, and let the pillow be before him, or directly in front of him, in height, so that the patient does not encounter labor.
Then let the surgeon extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage. And another surgeon shall extend similarly from above.
Then the doctor aligns the bone until he restores it to the best shape possible.
If there are fragments in the fractured bone, then endeavor to restore each piece to its place by your skill.
If there appears a separated piece and it pierces the skin, and you have no hope of restoring it: then incise over it, and remove it according to the method we have described previously.
If the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter on this, therefore take the treatment from there.
If a warm abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then line a cloth with cerote made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be between thick and thin: and bind the cloth tightly over it with a fine bandage until the abscess is calmed: then remove the cerote, and apply a plaster prepared from mill powder with egg white. Then place the splints, and let the splint that is placed over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And you shall know that the number of stars in the arm is six in most dispositions: whether the fracture be in one socket or in two sockets at once.
Then place your binding over the site of the fracture stronger and more forcefully, and somewhat more with the binding towards the upper part of the fracture, or towards the lower part of it: or place a milder binding for a while according to how the preceding recollection at the beginning of the chapter directs.
And let the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture be soft cloths, moist: and not hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is bound be of flax, properly medium between fine and coarse, as we have mentioned.
And consider the limb and the binding after days. And if something happens which requires correction, such as itching which occurs in the limb: then it is necessary to foment the limb with warmed water until that itching ceases, and leave the limb unconstricted until it rests, then bind it again.
And if the binding is already softened, and the bone already removed, and similar things to these: then correct all of it with your skill, and examine again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the binding, then it is necessary to soften it for a while and leave it for several days until the nourishment reaches it, then bind it again.
But if none of the things we have mentioned happen to the patient, then it is not necessary to remove the binding until after twenty days, or around this time.
Then suspend the patient's hand to his neck. And let his neck be straight, and be guarded by its strength from agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All of that depends on the dispositions of complexions, and the dispositions of strength.
On the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that fractures rarely occur in the knuckles of the hand and the joints of the fingers. And it does not happen to him, unless through frequent friction.
When therefore a fracture or wear occurs to the sole, then it is necessary that the patient sit upright: and before him there should be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand on it extended.
Then the servant extends the broken bones, and the doctor aligns them until they are joined properly.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, if a warm abscess does not occur. Then place a splint on top according to the length of the area: and you have now wrapped it in a linen cloth.
If however the fracture is near the lower part around the sole of the foot, then make something like a sphere from cloth: and instruct the patient to press upon it with his fractured foot: then bind it with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, in which there is flexibility, so that the leather may be applied with the body of the foot: and bind it according to what is necessary.
If however the fracture is on the outer side, then it is necessary to place a splint on top, and another splint below on the sole: and let the hand be placed straight and open. Then place the binding, as the hand is rotated: and extend between the fingers with a band.
If a fracture occurs to any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, align it according to what is necessary. Then bind it with the sole: and if you wish, place a straight, small splint on it, so that the fracture is raised and not moved.
If however the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, and index, or little, and ring finger, then align it and bind it with the healthy finger that follows it: or bind all according to continuity: and this is better. Or place on it a small splint, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the application of warm poultice: and oppose with what is necessary when something happens to it, according to how its description is repeated.
On the restoration of the fractured hip.
The hip bone is often fractured, and it is evident to the sense: for it turns to the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that a band is applied above the fracture, and another band below the fracture. And the patient shall lie down on his face.
Then the attendant shall stretch all the band to its part according to equality: and this, when there is a fracture in the middle of the bone.
If the fracture is near the root of the hip bone, then the band should be placed at the part of the pectineus muscle, so that the extension falls upward: and another band below the fracture.
And so similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then the band should be placed near the knee, so that the extension is downward.
Then the physician equalizes the bone with both of his hands until he restores it according to the similarity of its natural form: and the bone is joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and binding, unless the member has developed an abscess. If an abscess does occur: then leave it for several days until the warm abscess settles, then return to your treatment.
The binding of it should be done thus. You must wrap over the hard fracture, that is, the bandage: and double it two or three times: and let there be excess from it remaining. Then wrap the leg until the heel reaches the root of the thigh.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee to the lower part, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of both sides. Then wrap over the leg and thigh what is excess from the bandage.
Then place on the thigh at the site of the fracture the splints, and put one of them on top of the leg bone.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the binding is even.
Then begin with a strong binding from the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four wraps. And the farther you extend with the binding from the site of the fracture, let your binding be looser and softer, and gentler.
Then pull on the two ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and the leg. Bind therefore with both three splints which are on top. Then proceed with the two ends of the thread to the lower part until you reach the ankle. And with both pull also the ends of the splints from the other side: so that the binding does not move from its place.
Then release the binding on that while it remains, so that no corrosion occurs to the member, nor abscess, nor swelling, and things similar to these. If something happens from that. Hurry then with its solution, and correct everything that happens from it, according to what we have said many times.
If there is a fragment of bone in the bone that causes pain: then it is necessary to equally remove it, if it is possible for you. And if not, find on that and remove it: and treat it as previously mentioned until it is healed.
And sometimes this hip fracture is bound, besides adding splints to it, as we have said in the aid and arm.
However, in the restoration of such a fracture, the patient does not become lame. And if it is restored alone, besides applying the other leg to it, then it is necessary that the one to whom it belongs limps always.
And know that the hip is bound for fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is subtracted: all of this depends on the diversity of complexions and other conditions.
On the restoration of the fractured kneecap.
Know that fracture of the kneecap rarely occurs, but wear often happens to it.
If indeed a fracture occurs to it, then not otherwise than either a And it is with a wound, and without a wound. And it stands over the whole of it with sensation.
And the restoration of it is such that what has been torn apart from its parts is aligned with fingers until it is gathered together and joined as much as possible by alignment and ease, and construction.
Then apply a poultice of sesame flour or meal with egg white: and place a round splint on it if you need that: and bind over it with a suitable bandage for that.
Then consider all the treatments we have mentioned in other fractures: such as a warm abscess, and things like that: so as to oppose every accident with what is appropriate to it until it is healed.
On the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the legs are two bones, of which one is thick and is named the leg bone: and the other is slender and is named the fibula.
And it happens to both types of fractures as it happens to the two bones of the arm: and because of this, its restoration is like the restoration of the arm equally, and the procedure is the same.
If both bones are broken at the same time, the leg is turned in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is fractured, turn the leg to the front part.
And if the thicker bone is fractured, you find it below: and it is one of those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and equalization, and ligation of the splints according to it equally.
However, it is necessary, if the fracture of the leg is a nasty fracture, having many septic conditions: that there be less extension, and milder alleviation by you.
And in the leg there is something of the operation similar to the arm: and it is that when you equalize the splints, and complete all your operation, then take two bands from pine wood, which is used in making sun shades that are placed under boards: and they are made from palm ribs: and choose from them those which have a small thickness, and neither should they be from the thin ones: and their length should be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then wrap a cloth over each one with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and they should be from the knee down to the foot.
Then bind the two bands at three places from both ends and the middle. For when constriction is prohibited, the leg tilts to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And now a cradle made of wood according to the length of the leg should be administered, and the leg should be placed in it, so that it keeps it from movement.
And it is very important that you do this in a fracture when it is accompanied by a proper wound.
Then consider the leg throughout the day, and be diligent with the same last degree of care to prevent abscess, or swelling, or other similar issues. If any of those things happen, then apply to it what is necessary until it is healed, if God wills.
On the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
A complete fracture does not occur in the heel.
Fractures sometimes occur in the bones of the foot and rarely in the toes as well; and they do not suffer anything but abrasions in most cases.
If a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and you see those bones elevated above their natural position, then let the patient place his foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand and place your foot on the area that is elevated from those bones; then press or tread on it until it is leveled and returns to its proper position.
Then apply a plaster and place lint over it; and put a small board under the arch of the foot, which has two flat ends.
Then bind it to the foot with a suitable bandage after you wrap it with cloths and other necessary materials.
Therefore, after three or four days have passed, then loosen the binding. For you will find bones equal, whether they are broken or dislocated. Therefore, the operation on them is as you see.
If some fingers are broken, then restore them and align them as I have described to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint on the finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than it; then place under the foot the board I have described to you, and secure it with a suitable binding.
If however what is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place on every finger a splint of its size, wrapped in a linen cloth: and secure the board on the lower part of the foot: and let it stand on one end of the board outside the sole of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it is fitting for you, so that you do not forget, to consider all that I have told you about other fractures from the accidents we have mentioned: and address each accident with what is necessary.
Regarding the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the pelvis bone, and a man's virga.
When the vulva of a woman is fractured, then make her sit squarely. Then tilt her to the side of her back slightly, and hold her backward.
Then the midwife should fill her vulva with cotton until it is full: and it should form a sphere in the vulva from it.
Then shake the woman, and raise her back gradually. For that exits the gut until it becomes like a sphere in front of the vulva gate, and the broken mouth returns.
Then place the poultice on her back. When she wants to urinate, remove the gut easily, then urinate: and insert it back in the manner you first introduced it; then return to her original poultice.
Do this for seven days, or so, for the mouth itself is restored.
And if you wish, take the bladder of a sheep, and attach a reed tube to its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder inflates inside the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then it should be filled with gut and remain for several days, as we have mentioned, until it heals.
And when the mouth of the Pectinis of a man or woman is fractured, then administer for its restoration and alignment what we have mentioned for the Anchae bone.
And the rectum is not concealed in these nearby fractures that slightly affect it, especially in one who has some practice or use.
And understand my book well, for the fracture itself indicates to you the correct way in many arrangements for its restoration and binding: therefore, understand.
But when the man's penis is fractured, take the neck of a goose: and insert the rod into it. Then place over it a wrapping of cloth, and wrap it up, and leave it for three days, or around that time, and it will be healed, if God wills.
On the restoration of fractured bones when there is a wound.
It is appropriate for one to whom a fracture with a wound has happened, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone, or the auxiliary bone, or similar to it, that you should hurry and bleed him immediately: if the conditions for bleeding are indicated to you, as we have mentioned before.
If the wound is bleeding, then you must hurry to treat it by sprinkling ground zedoary over it: if you do not have anything else available.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not delay it, unless an abscess has occurred.
If an abscess does occur that is hot, then delay the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has settled. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause harmful complications to occur.
If the fractured bone protrudes above the skin: then you must endeavor to reduce it and equalize it with your hands with ease and a slight extension.
If however the extension and alignment of it are not prepared by your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument that is made of iron, whose length is seven fingers, or eight: and its width is according to the size of the wound. And for that reason, it is necessary for the doctor to take three or four of them according to the amount needed for that part of the treatment in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round: and let there be a small thickness in it, so that it does not double under pressure on it during the time of the operation: and let it have sharp ends, which have edges at its ends. And let the upper part of it be thickened from the middle of it down to the lower part.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation to place the sharp curved end of it on the protruding end of the bone: and push it with it once, until when the bone returns and aligns by some alignment.
Therefore, attempt to align the ends of the fracture closely together.
If the ends of it are finely fractured and do not receive good hold by the instrument, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it.
If you cannot reduce them at all with what we have mentioned, then cut it with one of the cutting instruments we have described that is suitable for it: or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
Then scrape what remains in the bone from roughness and tiny shreds.
When therefore the bone is reduced, and the patient finds after its reduction a very severe painful hurt, then you know that the bone did not return to its natural position.
Do therefore that. For you provide great assistance to the patient when the restoration you perform on the bone is completed.
Then dip the cloth in astringent, black wine. And especially if it is in summer. And you shall not place wax on the wound, nor anything containing oil: so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then apply the splints at the end of your treatment for the restoration of the bone. And leave the wound exposed: so that you cut with scissors a hole in the bandages according to the size of the wound.
And be cautious with all care, so as not to press the wound with the fracture. For foolish doctors have often done this: and it has caused death, or corruption, or pus, that is, suppuration, to occur in their patients. And let your binding be gentle, mild, different from the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is severe, or it is a large wound, and you fear some bad occurrences that we have mentioned might happen to it, and you find pain in the suspended area: then it should not be covered with splints. And make involutions from hard cloths at the site of the splinters, and bind it with them.
And when it is after one day, or two days, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove the cloth you placed over it with wine. Then apply licinia, and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds: such as the four-drug ointment, and those similar to it.
And it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage. And examine the wound every morning and evening until it strengthens and heals.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus may flow down to the lower parts with ease.
If many days pass by the wound and it does not strengthen, nor does the pus separate from it: then you know that there are small bone splinters therein. Therefore, it is necessary to examine the wound with a probe. How many of those splinters have been separated, remove them, and extract them. And those among them that are not separated and pierce the limb, causing pain, attempt to cut them out and remove them in any way possible for you.
If gangrene or ecchymosis, that is, discoloration, or other signs of corruption and putrefaction befall the wound: then it is necessary to oppose each of them with what is appropriate for it according to the treatment previously described in its chapter.
And from that in which it is necessary for my speech to stand, and to whom you make your mind present when the bone is broken, and protrudes above the limb, such as the hip bone, and helper bones, and similar ones from large limbs, is that you do not presume its attraction, for often death results from it, rather leave it until it putrefies.
For perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty days, or thirty.
Then therefore treat the wound if it seems right to you: and if not, leave it.
Regarding the treatment of nodulation in a fractured limb.
This nodulation often occurs in the limb during the healing of a fracture. Especially those near the joints: and the figure of the member is disfigured by it. And it may prevent the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, consider if the nodulation is recent: apply to it medications that have astringent properties, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, gum Arabic, acacia, and similar ones.
So that you take some of these or all of them: and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them on the nodulation on lint, and bind them tightly over it.
And leave the binding, and do not remove it for many days. Then untie it, and do another, until the knotting is removed.
Or apply it over the sheet of lead properly. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the members.
If the knotting has already turned into a stone and is tight, and necessity urgently requires its removal: then incise above it on the upper part of it, and cut off the prominent excess; or scrape it with some scrapers until it is removed; and care for it until it heals.
Regarding the treatment when it is restored, and a subtle member remains after it, not according to its nature.
When the fracture of the bone is restored, and a subtle, weak member remains after it: then this does not occur unless for many reasons. One of which is the multitude of loosening and tying of the bandage not as it should be: or due to the excess tightness of the bindings, until the nourishment is prevented from flowing to the member: or due to the abundance of excessive embrocation at an inappropriate time: or due to the lack of blood in the body of the sick person and their weakness.
