And the treatment for it is to feed the sick person and make their body tender until the blood multiplies in it. And administer a bath. And bring joy and happiness to him.
Then place pitch on the limb: so that the pitch may draw much nourishment to it. And continually apply a warm water rub to it until the nourishment circulates, and it returns to its natural form.
On the treatment of fractured bones when they heal crookedly.
When it happens that after the restoration and healing of a limb, there is a crookedness, prominence, or knotting of the fractured bone, and the form of the limb is marred by this, yet the limb is not hindered in its natural function: then you should not heed the advice of him who thinks the limb should be broken again.
And there have been many foolish doctors and restorers who have done this in our land.
And this operation is indeed very disgraceful, leading to death or great fear, and thus it is mortal.
But if the limping and knotting are recent: then it is necessary to apply a warm water rub, in which softening herbs have been decocted, such as the leaves of marshmallow and its root, and sweet clover, and similar things. And a poultice should be made for it with a softening plaster, such as properly prepared diachylon.
Or take the mucilage of the root of marshmallow, and mix it with chicken fat or sesamum oil: and apply a plaster with it.
Or take fat figs, and pound them with pigeon dung, and similar things which are called medicinal reducers of hardening.
And sometimes the knot is loosened with frequent, old, and gentle friction, which is done with wet hands. And the movement of the limb should be administered to all parts at all times.
If however the curvature has already preceded, and become strong, and transformed into stone, and the necessity for its treatment with iron has been provoked: then it is necessary that the upper part be cut, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and what is superfluous from the knot or bone be cut with delicate incisors: and ease be administered to it with care and attention.
Regarding the discussion on dislocation.
Dislocation is the departure of some joint from its place. Therefore, it is prohibited from its motion, and the figure of the member is disfigured. And it causes severe pains and injuries to the patient.
When therefore dislocation befalls someone, then it is necessary to hasten its restoration: do not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the area may become inflamed, and possibly the reduction of the dislocation with it.
Therefore, during the time of its inflammation, it should not be moved or extended. For it often causes spasms and painful injuries.
But when that happens, then you must hurry to bleed the patient. Then it should be left until the swelling rests.
Then the limb should be rubbed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And let the entire limb be treated with what will be mentioned in its proper place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocations, even as it precedes in fractures, from the upper part of the body to its lower part.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated slightly.
And the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways. For either they are moved from their places slightly, and are loosened for a moment. Or they are dislocated completely and entirely until they loosen toward the chest, until the patient's saliva flows and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
When the dislocation is slight, then it often returns to its proper position by itself with little intervention.
If it is a complete, entire dislocation, then the reduction must be administered quickly, and should not be delayed at all: and it is that the assistant hold the patient's head: and the physician inserts his thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw between the mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he should insert two of his thumbs at once, if the dislocation is from both parts. And the other fingers of his hand should be outside, with which he equalizes.
And instruct the sick person to clean their jaws and prepare them fully for eating on all sides. And the doctor should align the jaw and push it until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated at once: then apply evaporation with warm water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy: do not delay the reduction of both in any case, as we have said.
When they return and are aligned, and the mouth of the sick person is closed, and they do not soften: then place upon them small pieces of cloth with plaster made from wax and rose oil.
Then it should be bound easily with soft bands.
And let the sick person sleep on their back: and let their head stand between two pillows, so that they do not move it to the right or left: nor should they attempt or be forced to chew anything: rather, let their food be given by a gentle sip, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is bound: then let them eat what they want, and let it be administered easily.
Let them not strain their jaw during eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is bound and healed.
If the reduction of both jaws becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some time and do not return to their places: then often fevers and constant thirst result from this: and perhaps the sick person's stomach is loosened, and perhaps they vomit pure bile.
When therefore you see that: then know that it is a loss, and many times the one to whom this happens dies within ten days.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the humerus.
The collarbone does not dislocate from the inner side because of its connection with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates to the outer side: and this is apparent to the senses.
And its restoration is for the patient to lie on their back and extend their arms. Then force the area with your palm strongly, for it will return to its place.
Then apply a poultice on it, and bind it tightly.
The ends of it which follow the elbow and are connected to it do not dislocate, except rarely. If it dislocates at some time: then it is necessary to reduce and align it as we have said, and prepare it accordingly.
Then apply a poultice on it, and bind it tightly. And instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until healed.
And with this same method, the extremity of the humerus is reduced when it is also displaced from its position.
On the reduction of dislocation of the humerus.
Know that the humerus is not dislocated except in three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated towards the outer lower part. The second is that it is dislocated towards the chest. And it may also be dislocated towards the upper part of the humerus: and this happens rarely.
And it does not dislocate towards the back, because of the spur. And it does not dislocate towards the front, because of the nerves.
And it is dislocated and protrudes most towards the lower outer part, especially in those whose flesh is sparse. For it protrudes quickly in them and reduces quickly.
In those whose flesh is abundant, the opposite is true, namely that it protrudes with difficulty and reduces with difficulty.
And it may happen that some people suffer a blow or fall, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot swelling and is thought to be dislocated because of this.
Therefore, you must examine it until you stand on the certainty of it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is towards the lower outer part: and you distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy humerus. For you will find a clear difference among them, and you will find a concavity in the head of the humerus, and below the deltoid, upon touching the head of the humerus, it feels almost like an egg: and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all ways.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards: then you will find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easily done when it is recent, or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is for the assistant to raise the hand upwards. Then place your thumbs below its deltoid, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place: and the assistant raises his hand and extends it upwards. Then place it below, for it returns quickly.
If it does not return by what we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation first occurred: then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and a massage that softens and soothes should be administered, such as if the roots of hellebore, fenugreek, and sweet clover are boiled in water, and applied.
Then the patient should lie on his back: and a sphere and wool should be placed under his deltoid, which should be equal in softness and tightness.
Then the physician should place his heel on the sphere, and raise the head of the humerus with force, and pull the patient's hand downwards.
And if you wish, bring him back according to this method: that is, have a man who is taller than the sick person present, and let him stand on the side, and insert his shoulder under the armpit of the sick person: and lift his armpit upwards until the sick person is suspended in the air. And another servant should pull the hand of the sick person to the lower part of his abdomen. If the sick person is light: then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him to weigh him down: for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: that is, a long piece of wood should be fixed in the ground, whose head is round in shape: like the sleeve of a mortar: which should not be thick nor thin. Then place under the sick person after soft cloths are placed on top of the head of the wood: and let the sick person stand according to the length of the wood.
Then extend his hand downward from the other side, and stretch his body with force from the other side: for the joint returns to its place quickly.
If the reduction is difficult through all that we have said, then use this treatment: take a piece of wood whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness the quantity of two fingers: let it have a round head so that its entry into the armpit may be eased, according to this form.
Then soft cloths are bound over the round head so that the weak wood is not harmed.
Then it is placed under the patient's armpit, so that the hand or arm is extended over the wood down to the lower parts: and the wood is bound over the support and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a step of the ladder across its width, and the hand is extended downward, and the rest of the body is left suspended from the other side. For the joint immediately enters.
When therefore its entry is complete in whatever way it is possible: then it is necessary to place a sphere of equal size made of wool under the armpit. Then place a poultice prepared from mill dust with frankincense, and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it is applied from above.
Then bind under the armpit with a suitable bandage: and wrap the binding over the poultice from above: and attach his hand to his neck, and leave it, without moving his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that the patient's food be limited until the limb is strengthened: because it is faster for its healing.
Then it should be loosened after seven days, or five, and exercised with movement. If therefore it is firm and does not soften, then it is healed.
If however the joint dislocates frequently, due to moisture that affects it, or for some other reason: then it is necessary to apply a cautery with three holes to it, according to what was mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
If all this is done and the bandage is removed after seven days and the joint is not confirmed, and the plaster and binding are reapplied several times and it does not firm up, and falls off, and cannot lift itself upwards: then you know that his nerves which are at the shoulder head have already been cut, or extended, or softened: and you know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
Dislocation which occurs towards the chest and nipple, or reduction to the back, is performed with impulse and extension by hand: until it returns, and the rest of the binding and care are administered, until it is healed.
If after healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use the bath frequently until that heaviness is alleviated, and it returns to its original state.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty as well: and it is dislocated in all directions, and especially towards the front or the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: for it falls under sight and touch, regardless of whatever shape it is dislocated into. And when you compare the dislocated elbow with a healthy one, it is clearly evident to you. For the joint is concave, and it cannot bend the arm, nor can it touch its shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hurry and reduce the dislocation immediately before a warm abscess befalls him.
For if a warm abscess befalls him, the reduction becomes difficult: and perhaps it is not healed at all, especially when it is toward the back. For it is worse than all other kinds of dislocations, and they are of more severe pain: and often death comes with it.
And the restoration of it when it is one of those that can be reduced, is to extend his hand with two hands: and his arm should be spread out, and the doctor's hands should be above the elbow and below it, and he should push the joint with his thumbs simultaneously, or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or if the dislocation is to the front: then it returns thus, that he doubles his hand with his own hand, until he strikes the dislocated elbow with the base of his palm.
If the dislocation does not obey the reduction, then administer a very strong and powerful extension: and it is that two assistants extend the arm: and two assistants hold it as well, so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm should be rotated in all directions after a long doubled cloth or a wide bandage is wrapped over his hand.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with oil: so that it is one of those things that help in lubricating the joint with ease. Then push the joint with a strong force until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary to place on it a plaster that has astringency and drying with egg white: and bind it with a suitable bandage. And suspend the arm from the neck of the patient: and leave for several days.
Then it should be loosened. If therefore the joint is fixed in its place, then loosen the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not well bound, then apply the plaster again, and bind it with a bandage, and leave it for several days until it is bound. Then loosen it.
If however heaviness befalls him after the reduction of the joint, and the position is in motion: then administer moisture in bathing, and subtle friction, and compression, until it straightens, or place on the joint fat from the ilium, that is, the fatty tail of a ram. Then it should be bound and leave it on day and night. Then remove it, and immerse him in a bath. When therefore he sweats, then friction the joint with moderate friction. Then apply on it the juice of alelia once, and second, and third time with each bath until it is softened.
And if you wish, place cow dung on it, moist and heated with butter, and bind over it.
Do that several times. For it is relieved and returns to its normal state.
On the treatment of dislocation according to Albucasis.
The joint of the arm and hand is often dislocated, which is called Mahassem.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. On the contrary, of the other joints. However, it is necessary that the reduction of its dislocation be done quickly at the hour when it is dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed or a warm abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is to place the dislocated arm of the infirm hand on a board, and the assistant should extend his hand: and the doctor should place his palm on the prominence of the joint, and push it until it returns.
However, it should be considered if the palm has already receded to the belly of the hand: then the infirm should place the back of his hand on the board at the time of extension and reduction: and if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then the position of his hand should be inward on the board, that is, the inner part of his hand should be placed on the board so that the doctor's hand falls over the prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, that is good: and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaister to prevent inflammation: and leave it, do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you do anything about its reduction, after the days have passed, nevertheless the joint remains according to its crookedness: and it does not harm the sick person anything, except that the hand is weakened, and cannot grasp something: then know that the nerves are cut or worn away.
Therefore, there is no ingenuity in it, except to be cauterized near its origin. And perhaps it does not contribute anything to it.
Therefore, when Almahassem does not return, then apply to it the plaster which we have mentioned. Then it should be cauterized and left for five days: afterwards it should be loosened and left, and the hand should be exercised.
If however its movement is deprived, and something heavy happens to it: then soften it with warm water and friction several times until it is softened.
Regarding the treatment of the displacement of fingers.
When one of the fingers is dislocated in any direction, or one of the fingers to the back of the palm, or its front: then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the tip of the finger and suspend it toward the side to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it becomes straight on that day.
Therefore when it is night, bind it according to this account: and do not cease to loosen it during the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do this for several days until it tightens.
And similarly treat it if the hand is dislocated towards the abdomen: and bind it to that part, and perform your first treatment on it until it heals.
And similarly treat it when it is dislocated to other parts.
On the treatment of dislocations of the vertebrae.
When a complete dislocation of the vertebrae of the back or neck occurs, or when many vertebrae are displaced from their places, then there is no treatment for it, because death approaches the patient.
And the sign of this is that the patient's excretion escapes without will, and cannot hold it: and often some of his limbs are weakened, or both of his legs, or both of his arms, or one of them: or one vertebra is displaced from its place.
For it often happens that they are displaced to all four parts.
That which is displaced backwards is called humpback.
The treatment, however, is to consider then, if the humpback occurs from childhood: then there is no treatment for it, nor any healing at all.
In those that occur from a fall, or blow, or similar things, the ancients have already spoken at length about the methods of treatment. Of which much does not return with profit.
I have indeed abbreviated from that, of which little is excused from much due to its proximity to my intention: and in my explanation wherein I explained it, and formed its instrument in a different way from how they showed and explained it.
I say therefore, that in the humpback condition which occurs on the front side, there is no remedy, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which occurs on both sides as well.
And it is not cured by them, except that which occurs on the back properly, with what I narrate: and it is that the infirm should be extended on his face on an equal podium near the wall: and a wet straw should be spread under him, so that his chest is not injured.
Then a straight piece of wood fixed in a socket in the ground should be placed at the end of the podium near his head: and another piece of wood near his feet at the other end of the podium: and the assistant should hold the wood, and it should not be firmly fixed in the socket. And another assistant should hold the other one.
Then wrap over the head of the infirm and under his armpits a soft, strong band: and extend the end of the band to the wood that is by his head: and tie it there.
Then bind another band above his thighs, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bands together, and tie them to another stick that is at his feet.
Then let all the ministers extend the band to the part of the stick, and let not the two sticks be removed from the places in which they are fixed: however, they should not be fastened as we said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with force until it returns: or he may place a board on it. Then attach his feet to the board until it returns.
If it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And make a hole in the wall we mentioned that is near the patient, into which the end of the board can be inserted. Then place the middle of the board on the spine. And let the physician place his feet on the other end. And let him pull firmly until the spine is constricted and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a wrench that is wrapped with your hand: and it is, as you fix into the ground near the head of the patient at the end of the footrest two sticks whose length is one arm: and near his head at the end of the footrest two sticks.
And the length which is between all the wood should be one palm: and now a hole has been made everywhere where the turn runs: and all the wood should be firmly set in the ground very much, so that it does not move at all.
And a round wood is inserted: and it is a turn where the ligament is rotated in two holes of two woods.
And at its end is a hole, in which the wood is fixed, whose length is one palm with that which revolves: and in two other woods is similarly that.
Then tighten the ligaments which you tightened on the chest of the patient to the turn which is at his head: and the ligaments which you tightened on his legs to the turn which is at his feet.
Then let the assistant stand by every turn, who turns his hand with leather, with which the turn is rotated: and the doctor aligns the vertebrae according to what we have said.
This is the form of the turn that follows.
Then after the vertebrae return and the place is aligned, then it is necessary to apply a drying plaster with egg white. Then the tow. Then place over the plaster a strip of board, whose width is three fingers, or about that. And its length should be the amount that covers the hump and extends over the healthy vertebra part.
Then it should be bound with the bandage we mentioned. And let the patient use a subtle diet until he is healed.
And if something of the prominence remains in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, with the application of the plate we mentioned for a long time. And now it is applied with that sheet of lead.
And sometimes a prominence occurs in the last vertebrae of the spine: and it is thought that by it there is a dislocation, and it is that bone which now protrudes. Therefore, it should not be presumed to use this medicine on him: for it may cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
Know that the joints of the ankle and the shoulder joint do not occur except by dislocation only: nor does what happens to other joints due to slight displacement and concavity happen to them.
And the ankle joint dislocates in four directions. It is that it dislocates towards the front and towards the back.
And it dislocates most towards the inside: and slightly towards the front and the back.
And the treatment of its dislocation towards the inside is as follows: when you align the healthy leg of the patient with the injured one, it appears longer: and his knee protrudes more than that of a healthy person, and the patient cannot double his foot near the groin: and the area following the groin becomes inflamed with a warm swelling, because the head of the femur has now reached there.
And the sign of him to whom a dislocation outward has happened is that his symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And the sign of him to whom a dislocation forward has happened is that he stretches the healthy leg according to its fullness: however, he does not double it without pain which is in the knee: and if he attempts to walk, he cannot step forward on it: and his urine is retained, and his groin swells, and in walking there is a dragging of his heel.
And the sign of him to whom a dislocation backward has happened is that he cannot straighten the knee, nor can he double it before doubling the groin: and his leg is even shorter than the other: and the groin is softened, and the head of the femur is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of these types of dislocations is to consider then, if the dislocation is old, which already has been present for some time with the one having it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains according to its position: then there is no cure in it at all. Therefore, it should not be entirely presumed for him.
In the case of one whose dislocation is recent, and it is from one of the four species of dislocation: then hurry to this, to rotate the joint, and extend it inward and outward, and move it right and left: for perhaps it will return, and will not require further treatment.
If it does not return this way, then you must prepare a strong assistant, and have him extend his leg from the lower part: either with his hands or with a bandage, which should be tied above his leg over the knee: and another assistant should reach over and place his hands under the ligaments. Then bind with a gentle bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the end of the bandage with the other hand.
And let the extension be either from the front part of the groin to the shoulder part, or from the back part to the back area.
And let the extension of all be at once, until the sick person is lifted with their body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common type among the four types.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good: and if not, then it is necessary for every type to do what I will say about the proper cure.
In its proper reduction when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary for the sick person to lie on their healthy side. Then place the bandage over the root of the thigh, between the head of the femur and the area beneath the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin area to a higher part of the body, toward the shoulder area.
Then let another strong assistant take his arms, and compress the thick part of the diseased thigh, and extend outward with a forceful stretch. For it now returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member is reduced.
If it slips out of your control and does not obey you for entry with this type of treatment at all, then it is necessary to bind both feet of the patient simultaneously with a strong, gentle bandage over both heels and both knees: and let the length of each be equal in quantity to four fingers; and let the sick leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then let the patient be suspended over a beam in the house: and its length from the ground should be two arms' lengths.
Then command a strong youth to support the head of the thigh: and let him be suspended with the patient; and another youth should push, supporting the thigh with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
In returning it to its proper place, when the dislocation is outward, it is necessary that the patient lie on a raised platform, as we have described in the case of one having a hump. And a band should be applied above his diseased leg and on his chest.
Then two beams should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then a beam should be added in the middle of the raised platform, firmly fixed: on which wrapped wet cloths should be placed, so that it does not harm the patient: and there should be a beam between his thighs, so that it is not pulled downward during extension.
Then let all the attendants pull at their respective parts, and the doctor equalize the reduction with his hand.
If it obeys the return, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the board and press it as we said in the case of hunchback, equally. However, it is necessary that the patient lies on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is forward, then it is necessary that the patient's leg be extended quickly: and this is according to the same description on the raised platform: and the doctor should place his right hand palm on the patient's groin. Then compress it with the other hand, and with that compression place it extended downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is towards the back: then it should not be extended downwards, and he should be raised off the ground: rather, he should be placed on a hard surface, as should be done also if his hip is dislocated outward, according to what we have said about lying on two hips: and he is on his face, and the ligaments are stretched: according to what we have recently said.
And it is necessary to apply compression with a board also on the place to which the joint protrudes, when the reduction is completed as required.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the two legs of the patient: and when you see them equal, and the patient can pull his leg and stretch it without difficulty: then know that the limb has returned as required: then join the two hips, and apply a plaster, and bind with an imemati, that is, a wheel: so that the hip does not move in any direction: and let the patient rest quietly for three days or four.
Then loosen the bandage and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in standing, then know that the dislocation is now fixed: therefore allow the patient to walk.
And if you see any softening: then return, and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding: and leave it for three days as well.
Then loosen it and make it slow in walking on both for several days until it is well strengthened.
About the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outward, inward, and downward, that is, backward.
And it does not dislocate forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you instruct the patient to apply his leg to his hip. If it does not adhere to the hip, then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of its dislocation is to make the patient sit straight, so that he may extend his leg if he can over it, and place your back against his face: and put his foot between your feet.
Then hold his knee with your palms and place them straight across his knee over your fingers. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another assistant should extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the hip easily, not with difficulty.
Then apply plasters to it, and make the leg adhere to the hip. Then bind them together with wool for three days, or four. Then loosen them.
And the patient shall not walk for several days until healed.
If the reduction is refused to you, then administer a strong extension with bandages, of which the method was previously described in the treatment of anchae, until it returns.
Regarding the treatment of dislocation of the calcaneus.
The calcaneus is sometimes slightly displaced and sometimes dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the calcaneus swollen, protruding to the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment for its slight displacement is easy restoration: it is to extend it gently with the hands and equalize it until it returns.
In treating it when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary to have the patient sit upright: and a strong assistant should hold him firmly from behind, at the middle of his back.
Then hold with your right hand the foot from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot where the heel is.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left twice.
Then extend with the left, and push the chest of the foot with the right towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have said.
Then push the chest of the foot towards the leg on the third try, and you pull the awl.
If it returns in one try, or in two tries according to this method, and you see the foot even, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation on it: because it returns.
If the reduction of it is prevented by what we have mentioned: then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and fix a stake in the ground very firmly, so that it falls between his thighs: and now you have wrapped a cloth over it, so that it does not hurt the patient.
Then the assistant should take hold of his thigh: afterwards the assistant should extend the other foot, either with his hands or with a band that ties above the ankle. Then the assistant should extend fully against the extension of his colleague: and the stake should stand between the two thighs of the patient holding him so that his body is not pulled downwards during the extension.
Then the doctor equalizes the dislocation with his hands, and another assistant holds the healthy leg downwards. For the dislocation returns quickly.
When the dislocation returns and its proper healing is evident to you: then apply a plaster and tow, and bind it firmly with bandages, and tie the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And you must be cautious or fearful of the nerves that are above the posterior heel, so that there is not a strong binding over them and injures them.
Then leave it for two days or three. If the binding softens, tighten it; then loosen it on the third or fourth day: and prevent the patient from walking for forty days. If however he attempts to walk before that period, there is no security that the dislocation over it will not dissolve, be corrupted, and not receive treatment afterward.
If a warm abscess should occur to him, then it is necessary to apply for its reduction what we have previously described elsewhere in the treatment, and fomentation, until it is removed.
Regarding the dislocation of the toes and its treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated among them be aligned with slight extension without effort: and this indeed is not difficult, but rather easy.
If the dislocation is in some of the joints of the foot's arch: then it is necessary for the patient to sit with his foot on an even place on the ground, or on a board: and he should stand as if walking.
Then you stand and place your foot on that which protrudes from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force until it returns and you see it now even, and no protrusion appears in its place.
Then place under his foot's belly a board which takes the whole foot, which has two heads. Then bind it tightly for three days. Then loosen it, and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is healed and secured from displacement.
Regarding the types of dislocations with wounds or fractures.
When something of that kind occurs and you attempt its treatment and restoration, death often follows: and therefore one should not presume to treat such a condition unless he is wise in the art through long experience, merciful, pious, cautious, not bold, nor presumptuous: and that he use at the outset medicines which calm inflammations, only warm ones: and leave the patient to divine judgment. Unless you think or hope for salvation from peril with the mildness of the illness, and in which there appears some hope. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a warm inflammation occurs.
If the limb returns as we wish, then administer a treatment that calms the inflammation: and treat the wound with what is appropriate from drying ointments.
If the dislocation is with a fracture, and separate fragments occur in the bone, then attempt their removal, and do what we have said for simple injuries resulting from them in their respective locations.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the way of fear: according to which my preceding instruction was given to you.
For it is better for you with your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and safer for your body among them during your time.
